Defending Logic Using al-Ghazali's Authority
Author defends logic against critics, citing Ghazali's endorsement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the author's defense of logic against the objection that al-Ghazali rejected it, and evaluate the rhetorical strategy of citing authority.

Core Idea
Logic is indispensable for Islamic sciences; even al-Ghazali affirmed it after his Sufi turn.

This passage confronts a common doubt: why study logic if a great scholar like al-Ghazali seemed to abandon it? The author argues that al-Ghazali's later work al-Mustasfa actually placed logic as the foundation of all sciences. Understanding this defense is crucial for appreciating the traditional role of logic in Islamic scholarship and for responding to similar criticisms today.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Find the sentence where the author mentions that some weak-minded think logic is useless because al-Ghazali turned to Sufism. This is the objection being addressed.
The Arabic phrase 'وقد يفكر بعض الضعاف لما ذا المنطق ؟ والغزالي...' signals the objection.
2
Locate the evidence
Look for the author's key evidence: al-Ghazali's statement from al-Mustasfa. The author quotes that logic is the introduction to all sciences and that whoever does not master it cannot be trusted. Note how this quote directly refutes the objection.
The quote is: 'جعل المنطق مقدمة العلوم كلها، ومن لا يحيط بها فلا ثقة له بعلومه أصلا.'
3
Analyze the rhetorical strategy
Beyond the quote, the author uses strong language against critics (e.g., 'المتخنث المترجل المتهور') and claims logic has survived seven centuries. Consider how this combination of authoritative citation and dismissal of opponents strengthens the defense.
This appeal to authority and ad hominem is common in polemical works.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إبطال احتمال وجود دليل آخر عليه عرفناه أو لم نعرفه، ففي ظل هذا الاحتمال استحال إبطال صحة المدلول ، حتى وإن لم يتم إقامة دليل آخر عليه في مجلس المناظرة فرضا فلا ينتقض المدلول بذلك؛ لمجرد أن الأمة تلقته بالقبول عبر العصور ، فهو عين الدليل. لقد مضى على صيحات نقض المنطق ما يقرب من سبعة قرون، إلا أنه لم ينتقض بعد، ولم تتم إقامة بديل أراده ليشيد فوقه فلسفة الحشو، بل هو من فشل إلى فشل، لم تفرض الأمة سُنَّته ، بل رفضوا بدعته. وأصحاب المنهج ما زالوا متمكنين من بيان صحة المعتقد أمام كل طوائف المخالفين، وثنيين، أو ملحدين، أو ماديين أو دهريين أو علمانيين، أو أهل بدعة مختلفين. يستخدمون المنطق في تحقيق هذا الهدف، علموا مواضع الخلل فيه أو لم يعلموا ، طالما لم يأت - ولن يأتي - الفوضويون بمنهج يقاربه، فضلا عما يساويه، فضلا عما هو أحسن منه. وفي اليوم الذي ينبعث المجادلون بما يساويه نجادلهم بالتي هي أحسن مما جاؤوا به ، إن شاء الله . وقد يفكر بعض الضعاف لما ذا المنطق ؟ والغزالي رحمة الله بين عدم فائدته ، وانتهى به الأمر إلى التصوف ، وصنف للصوفية أعظم دستور يعتزون به ويسيرون في ضوئه، به إحياء علوم الدين، والوصول إلى اليقين. إن هذا الخاطر قلما ينجو منه طالب ؛ حيث الشيطان يراود كل إنسان على حسب طريقته ، فالذي يرى صحة مذهب أهل السنة يأتيه الشيطان بمثل هذه الهواجس. فليخبر شيطانه بأن الإمام الغزالي رحمة الله ألف بعد هذا «الإحياء» كتابا كبيرا هو من أعظم الكتب في أصول الفقه، وفي مقدمته تكلم إمام الصوفية هذا في علم المنطق وعنه بكلام سلس رائع ، بل فيه قال قولته الشهيرة التي تعد من الكلمات المغيرة لمسار التاريخ في الفكر الإسلامي؛ حيث جعل المنطق مقدمة العلوم كلها، ومن لا يحيط بها فلا ثقة له بعلومه أصلا. هنا يجب دراسة هذا العلم بإتقان ودقة، ويجب معرفة كيفية ومن استخدامه في إثبات المطالب الدينية ، كما يجب أن يتسم النظر فيه نظرا نقديا قائما على المنهج العقلي السليم، لا النقد الأعمى، حتى إذا تبين وجود خلل الاستدلال . هذا فيه يستطيع تداركه ،وإصلاحه، ويتم بناء صرح منهج هو الذي فعله أئمتنا فيما مضى من الزمن، أضافوا إليه، وحذفوا منه، وما أضافوه إليه وحذفوه منه نظر فيه من جاؤوا بعدهم، فوافقوهم عليه طورا، وطورا رفضوه، وموافقة هؤلاء لسابقيهم ومخالفتهم إياهم أيضا تعرضت لنقد اللاحقين، قبولا أو رفضا وهكذا العملية تمضي قدما إلى الأبد، لكن بشرط أن يكون الناقد أهلا للنقد، فلا نبالي بهمسات أناس لا يعرفون قواعد الصرف والنحو، فضلا عن المنطق ، ثم يعترضون على ما قرره علماء هذا الفن، مدعيا أنه رجل كما هم رجال، كلا أبدا، بل هو المتخنث المترجل المتهور، يصير لا محالة إلى ما صار إليه أمثاله في مزبلة التاريخ
Nullifying the possibility of the existence of another proof for it, whether we know it or not. For under this possibility, it becomes impossible to invalidate the correctness of the conclusion, even if another proof is not presented in the debate session hypothetically, the conclusion is not invalidated by that; merely because the ummah accepted it through the ages, for it is the very proof. Nearly seven centuries have passed since the cries to refute logic, yet it has not been refuted, nor has an alternative been established that he wanted to build the philosophy of redundancy upon it; rather it goes from failure to failure. The ummah did not impose his sunnah, but rejected his innovation. The adherents of the methodology are still able to demonstrate the correctness of belief before all sects of opponents, polytheists, atheists, materialists, heretics, secularists, or various people of innovation. They use logic in achieving this goal, whether they know its defects or not, as long as the anarchists have not come—and will not come—with a methodology that approaches it, let alone equals it, let alone is better than it. And on the day when the disputants come with what equals it, we will dispute them with that which is better than what they brought, God willing. Some weak-minded may think, why logic? And al-Ghazali, may God have mercy on him, explained its uselessness, and he ended up with Sufism, and composed for the Sufis the greatest constitution they take pride in and follow, namely Ihya Ulum al-Din, and attainment of certainty. This thought rarely a student escapes; for Satan tempts every person according to his way. So one who sees the correctness of the Sunni school, Satan comes to him with such misgivings. Let him inform his Satan that Imam al-Ghazali, may God have mercy on him, after this 'Ihya' authored a great book that is among the greatest books in Usul al-Fiqh, and in its introduction, this Imam of Sufism spoke about logic eloquently, indeed he said his famous statement that is considered among the world-changing words in Islamic thought: He made logic the introduction to all sciences, and whoever does not master it has no trust in his sciences at all. Here we must study this science with mastery and accuracy, and we must know how to use it in proving religious claims, and the consideration of it must be critical, based on sound rational methodology, not blind criticism, so that if a flaw in reasoning appears, he can address it and correct it, and a structure of methodology is built, which is what our imams did in past times: they added to it and deleted from it, and what they added and deleted was considered by those who came after them, sometimes agreeing, sometimes rejecting, and their agreement or disagreement also underwent criticism by successors, acceptance or rejection, and thus the process proceeds forever, but on condition that the critic is qualified for criticism. So we do not care about whispers of people who do not know the rules of morphology and syntax, let alone logic, then they object to what the scholars of this art have established, claiming he is a man just as they are men. No, never! Rather he is the effeminate, the pretender, the reckless, inevitably heading to where his likes have gone in the garbage dump of history.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage is a vigorous defense of logic against its detractors, embedded in a commentary on logic. The author begins by arguing that the mere possibility of another proof does not invalidate an established conclusion; the ummah's acceptance over centuries itself constitutes proof. He notes that despite nearly seven centuries of attempts to refute logic, it remains unrefuted and no alternative has been provided. The defenders of the methodology still use logic effectively against all opponents. He anticipates a common objection: if al-Ghazali, after writing Ihya, seemed to reject logic, why study it? The author clarifies that al-Ghazali later wrote al-Mustasfa, a major work on usul al-fiqh, in whose introduction he famously declared logic the prerequisite for all sciences— 'whoever does not master it has no trust in his sciences at all.' The author then exhorts the serious study of logic with precision, using it critically and constructively, while dismissing unqualified critics as ignorant and heading for the 'garbage dump of history.'

Key Terms
المنطق
إحياء علوم الدين
المستصفى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Ghazali's famous statement in al-Mustasfa, what is the status of logic for the sciences?
A) Logic is optional for those studying theology.
B) Logic is the introduction to all sciences, and whoever does not master it has no trust in his sciences.
C) Logic is only useful for philosophy, not Islamic sciences.
D) Logic was rejected by al-Ghazali after writing Ihya.

Key Takeaway
The author successfully defends logic by invoking al-Ghazali's later endorsement and contrasting it with critics' ignorance, showing that logic remains essential for Islamic scholarship.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Write a brief defense of logic, using at least one authoritative Islamic scholar's statement (besides al-Ghazali) that affirms the importance of logic for Islamic sciences. For instance, you might use Ibn Khaldun's or al-Shatibi's comments on logic.

Analysing a Refutation (Radd) in Islamic Scholarship
Author refutes a claim that certain Indian scholars rejected kalam and mantiq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key components of a refutation (radd) in Islamic scholarly writing and evaluate the sufficiency of evidence presented.

Core Idea
A refutation requires counter-evidence from the opponents' own works, not mere denial.

When a scholar is accused of abandoning a tradition, the most effective refutation is to show that the scholar's own writings contradict the accusation. In this passage, the author defends a group of Indian scholars against the claim that they rejected 'ilm al-kalam and al-mantiq. He does so not by arguing abstractly, but by quoting their creedal statements, listing their works, and even citing their poetry. This lesson will help you break down the structure of such a refutation and assess its strength.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being refuted
Read the opening sentence where the author states what others allege: that certain Indian scholars abandoned the traditional madhhab, rejected kalam and mantiq, and invented a strange doctrine.
Notice that the author calls it a 'strange doctrine' (madhhab gharib), indicating disapproval.
2
Extract the author's counter-arguments
The author lists several points: (1) the scholars' students did not know of any departure; (2) the scholars themselves declared Ash'ari creed in their books; (3) they wrote many works on kalam and mantiq; (4) they engaged in tawassul, which the accusers consider shirk.
These are distinct types of evidence: testimony of students, textual creedal statements, bibliographic evidence, and practical evidence from poetry.
3
Examine the textual evidence provided
Look at the quotes from Shah Wali Allah's 'Khaza'in al-Hikmah' and 'Rasa'il al-Tafhimat al-Ilahiyyah'. Identify which Ash'ari positions they affirm (e.g., attributes are eternal, no origination in essence).
Note that the author uses the scholar's own words, not third-party reports.
4
Assess the refutation's strength
Consider whether the evidence sufficiently disproves the claim. Does it cover both creed and practice? Are the quotes representative? Is there any counter-evidence the author might have omitted?
A strong refutation addresses the core accusation directly and uses primary sources.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنهم كانوا هم المبشرين الأوائل بهذا المذهب الغريب في الهند ولم يكونوا على المذهب التقليدي - الذي هو مذهب الأشاعرة والماتريدية كما لا يخفى ، ووجوب التقليد لأحد الأربعة في الفقه - وأنهم تركوا علم الكلام والمنطق، وأنهم وأنهم... ويتشبثون في ذلك بنقول من كتبهم، يحسبون أنها تساند رأيهم. وهذه الدعوى وإن كانت بحاجة إلى حديث مفصل، ورد موسع ، بذكر النصوص وتوثيق النقول ؛ حتى لا تقوم للعشوائية قائمة ، إلا أن هذه العجالة لا تسمح بذلك ، ونكتفي هنا ببعض الإشارات التي تفك هذه العقدة. والرد عليها باختصار: أن هؤلاء إن كانوا على خلاف الأشعرية والماتريدية فلم لم يعرف ذلك أقرب تلاميذهم ؟ بل الذين حملوا عنهم العلم والطريقة كانوا على المذهب التقليدي ! بل إن هؤلاء العظماء أنفسهم صرحوا بعقيدة الأشعري أو الماتريدي في كتبهم ومن هنا تأخرت هذه الدعوى (1) (۱) هذا هو الشاه ولي الله الدهلوي يقول مثلا في كتابه خزائن الحكمة» (١٤٣ - ١٤٥) :: «ولمذهب الشيخ أبي الحسن عندنا وقع ، ومذهبه من تماثيل مذهب الصحابة ... وبالجملة لو اعتبرت الحالة التي تحق بالصحابة فلا تحقيق إلا في مذهب الأشاعرة، التي تجب على المقلدين ، فكل فرقة مقلدة أبت ذلك فهي خاطئة . . وهذه الحالة هي ويقول أيضا في كتابه رسائل التفهيمات الإلهية» (١٣٥) :: ... ولا يقوم بذاته حادث ، فليس في ذاته ولا في صفاته حدوث، وهو بريئ عن الحدوث والتجدد من جميع الوجوه .... ولا يشار إليه هنا أو هناك، ولا يصح عليه الحركة والانتقال والتبدل في ذاته ولا في صفاته ....» ، ويقول فيها أيضا: فليس في ذاته حدوث ، وإنما الحدوث في تعلق الصفات بمتعلقاتها»، وقال في خزائن الحكمة» (۱۰۰): «ولما أُورد على إمام أهل السنة أن صفات الله قديمة، فلم حدث الكلام ؟ تفصى عنه بأن الصفة قديمة، وتعلقها حادث».
ثم إن كانوا يكرهون علم الكلام بل نشروا علم الحديث النبوي الشريف - فقط - فلما ذا هذا الكم من الكتب الكلامية والمنطقية التي تركها كل واحد منهم لمن بعدهم؟ ولِمَ لَمْ يبتعد أساطين أهل العلم في الهند من كالمحب البهاري وبحر العلوم مثلا - عن هذه الطريقة التي نبذوها بعدهم - وكانوا جميعا ممن يعظمون السرهندي ويلقبونه على حسب زعمهم بـ (مجدد الألف الثاني ، كما أن الشاه ولي الله إمام يقتدى به عندهم! بل غاصوا في تلك العلوم العقلية وعاموا وأبدعوا في تصنيف الأصول، وتفننوا وانظر ما نقله صاحب «الثقافة الإسلامية في (الهند) (٤٦) عن الدهلوي من أبيات طنانة رنانة، فيها التوسل والاستشفاع بالحبيب صَلَّى اللَّهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ ، الذي اتهم سواد الأمة بالشرك والكفر بسببه ، وأخرجوا من الملة، يقول فيها الدهلوي رحمه الله: كأن نجوما أومضت في الغياهب عيون الأفاعي أو رؤوس العقارب إذا كان قلب المرء في الأمر خاثرا فأضيق من تسعين رحب السباسب وتشغلني عني وعن كل راحتي مصائب تقفو مثلها في المصائب إذا ما أتتني أزمة مدلهمة تحيط بنفسي من جميع الجوانب تطلبت هل من ناصر أو مساعد ألوذ به من خوف سوء العواقب فلست أرى إلا الحبيب محمدا رسول إله الخلـق جـم ومعتصم المكروب في كل غمرة ومنتجع الغفران من كل هائب ملاذ عباد الله ملجأ خوفهم إذا جاء يوم فيه شيب الذوائب
That they were the first heralds of this strange doctrine in India and they were not upon the traditional madhhab - which is the madhhab of the Ash'aris and Maturidis, as is not hidden, and the obligation of taqlid of one of the four in fiqh - and that they abandoned kalam and mantiq, and that they, and that they... and they cling in that to quotations from their books, thinking that they support their view. And this claim, although it needs a detailed discussion and a wide refutation, with mention of texts and documentation of quotations, so that no room is left for haphazardness, yet this brief note does not permit that, and we suffice here with some indications that untie this knot. The refutation of it in brief: If these [people] were contrary to the Ash'aris and Maturidis, then why did their closest students not know that? Rather, those who carried knowledge and the method from them were upon the traditional madhhab! Indeed, these great ones themselves declared the creed of al-Ash'ari or al-Maturidi in their books, and thus this claim is belated. (1) This is Shah Wali Allah al-Dihlawi, saying for example in his book Khaza'in al-Hikmah (143-145): 'The madhhab of al-Shaykh Abi al-Hasan has weight with us, and his madhhab is like the madhhab of the Companions... In summary, if one considers the state that befits the Companions, then there is no verification except in the madhhab of the Ash'aris, which is obligatory upon the muqallids, so every muqallid group that refuses that is mistaken.' And this state is... And he also says in his book Rasa'il al-Tafhimat al-Ilahiyyah (135): '...No originated thing subsists by His essence, so there is no origination in His essence nor in His attributes, and He is free from origination and renewal from all aspects... and He is not pointed to here or there, and movement, transition, and change do not apply to His essence nor to His attributes.' And he also says in it: 'There is no origination in His essence, rather origination is in the attachment of attributes to their objects.' And he said in Khaza'in al-Hikmah (100): 'When it was objected to the Imâm of Ahl al-Sunnah that the attributes of God are eternal, so why did speech occur? He extricated himself by [saying] that the attribute is eternal, and its attachment is originated.' Then if they disliked kalam and only spread the noble prophetic hadith, then why this quantity of kalam and mantiq books that each one of them left for those after them? And why did the masters of knowledge in India, like al-Muhibb al-Bahari and Bahr al-Ulum for example, not distance themselves from this method which they rejected after them? And they all used to venerate al-Sirhindi and title him, according to their claim, as 'Mujaddid al-Alf al-Thani', just as Shah Wali Allah is an Imam followed by them! Rather, they plunged into those rational sciences, swam, and excelled in composing usul, and refined [their skills]. And look at what the author of 'al-Thaqafah al-Islamiyyah fi al-Hind' (46) narrated from al-Dihlawi of resounding verses, containing tawassul and seeking intercession with the Beloved ﷺ, for which the black mass of the ummah was accused of shirk and kufr, and were expelled from the millah. Al-Dihlawi, may Allah have mercy on him, says in them: 'As if stars flashed in the darkness, eyes of snakes or heads of scorpions. When one's heart is weak in the matter, then it is narrower than ninety broad wildernesses. And afflictions preoccupy me from myself and from all my comfort, following like them upon afflictions. When a dark crisis comes to me, encompassing my self from all sides, I seek: Is there any helper or assistant that I may cling to from fear of evil consequences? I see nothing but the beloved Muhammad, the Messenger of the God of all creation, and the refuge of the distressed in every calamity, and the resort of forgiveness from every fearful one, the refuge of the servants of Allah, the shelter of their fear when a day comes in which the forelocks go grey.'
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of a claim made against certain Indian scholars, likely the Deobandis or similar groups, that they abandoned the traditional Ash'ari-Maturidi creed, rejected kalam and mantiq, and invented a strange new doctrine. The author begins by characterizing the claim: that these scholars were the first to propagate this strange doctrine in India, were not on the traditional madhhab, rejected kalam and mantiq, and rely on selective quotes from their books. The author says the claim requires a detailed response but he will provide brief indications. He then gives the refutation in summary: if these scholars were contrary to Ash'ari and Maturidi, why did their closest students not realize that? Actually, their students followed the traditional madhhab. Moreover, these great scholars themselves declared adherence to Ash'ari or Maturidi creed in their books, as evidenced by quotes from Shah Wali Allah al-Dihlawi. The author then cites Shah Wali Allah's statement from Khaza'in al-Hikmah affirming the Ash'ari madhhab and another from Rasa'il al-Tafhimat al-Ilahiyyah denying origination in God's essence and attributes, and yet another explaining that speech is eternal with its attachment originated—a clear Ash'ari position. The author then asks rhetorically: if they disliked kalam and promoted only hadith, why did they produce so many kalam and mantiq books? Why did masters like al-Muhibb al-Bahari and Bahr al-Ulum (the author himself) not abandon this method? In fact, they revered Ahmad al-Sirhindi as the 'Mujaddid of the Second Millennium' and followed Shah Wali Allah, and they delved deeply into rational sciences, writing extensively on usul. Finally, the author presents poetry from Shah Wali Allah showing tawassul and istishfa' with the Prophet, contradicting the accusation that they considered such acts as shirk. The verses express seeking refuge with the Prophet in times of distress. Thus, the author argues that the claimed stance is inconsistent with the actual writings and practices of these scholars.

Key Terms
المذهب التقليدي
علم الكلام
المنطق
الشاه ولي الله الدهلوي
التوسل

Checkpoint
What is the author's primary method of refuting the claim that Indian scholars abandoned kalam and mantiq?
A) Citing the scholars' own writings that show their adherence to Ash'ari creed and production of kalam works.
B) Arguing that the accusers are ignorant of Indian history.
C) Relying on the authority of later scholars who condemned the claim.
D) Using rational arguments to prove the necessity of kalam.

Key Takeaway
An effective refutation in Islamic scholarship uses primary evidence from the accused's own writings to counter claims of deviation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find a secondary source that accuses a classical Islamic scholar of holding a heretical view. Then locate the scholar's own writings to see if the accusation holds. Write a brief refutation using the same method: quoting the scholar's actual words.
?
In the passage, the author uses multiple types of evidence. Identify which type (creedal statement, bibliographic, poetic) you think is strongest and explain why in one paragraph.

Detecting Contradictions in Claims About Rejecting Rational Sciences
Author critiques a claim that Indian scholars rejected kalam and logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a factual contradiction between a claim and the evidence, using the example of the alleged rejection of rational sciences by Indian scholars.

Core Idea
A claim is disproven by the existence of works that contradict it.

In this passage, the author confronts a paradoxical claim: that certain Indian scholars rejected rational sciences like kalam and logic, yet they themselves authored substantial works in those fields. This contradiction serves as a powerful tool for evaluating the truth of any historical or doctrinal assertion. The lesson will guide you through identifying such contradictions and assessing their implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being attacked
What is the specific claim that the author is refuting? Look for the statement about Indian scholars' attitude toward rational sciences. The author mentions a claim that they detested kalam and only propagated hadith.
2
Find the evidence that contradicts the claim
What does the author point to as evidence that the claim is false? The author asks why there are so many theological and logical books left by these scholars if they truly rejected those fields.
The evidence is the existence of their own works.
3
Analyze the logical structure of the contradiction
How does the contradiction invalidate the claim? The author argues that if they truly rejected rational sciences, they would not have produced such works. The presence of the works contradicts the claim, so the claim must be false.
This is a simple logical refutation by contradiction.
4
Evaluate the author's conclusion
The author concludes that the claim is false and fabricated. Is this conclusion logically justified based on the evidence presented? Yes, the contradiction between the claim and the observable evidence (the books) is a strong reason to reject the claim.
One might consider whether the works could have been produced before the alleged rejection, but the author assumes they represent the scholars' full output.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن كانوا يكرهون علم الكلام بل نشروا علم الحديث النبوي الشريف - فقط - فلما ذا هذا الكم من الكتب الكلامية والمنطقية التي تركها كل واحد منهم لمن بعدهم؟ ولِمَ لَمْ يبتعد أساطين أهل العلم في الهند من كالمحب البهاري وبحر العلوم مثلا - عن هذه الطريقة التي نبذوها بعدهم - وكانوا جميعا ممن يعظمون السرهندي ويلقبونه على حسب زعمهم بـ (مجدد الألف الثاني، كما أن الشاه ولي الله إمام يقتدى به عندهم! بل غاصوا في تلك العلوم العقلية وعاموا وأبدعوا في تصنيف الأصول، وتفننوا فلا نستطيع التخلص من هذه التناقضات العجيبة في ضوء هذه الدعوى الغريبة، إلا بالحكم عليها بأنها كاذبة لم تَنْبَنِ على أساس يثبت صدقها، بل مما اخترعتها عقولهم الماكرة ؛ ليروجوا بها بضاعتهم التي لولا مثلها لن تَجِدَ إلى رواجها سبيلا ، ولكن الله متم نوره ولو كره الملبسون .
Then if they detest the science of kalam and only propagate the noble prophetic hadith, then why this abundance of theological and logical books that each one of them left for those after them? And why did the great scholars of knowledge in India, like al-Muhibb al-Bihari and Bahr al-Ulum for example, not abandon this method which they repudiated after them? And they all were among those who venerated al-Sirhindi and titled him, according to their claim, as 'the Renewer of the Second Millennium', and also Shah Wali Allah is an imam to be followed according to them! Rather, they plunged into those rational sciences, swam in them, innovated in writing on fundamentals, and excelled. So we cannot extricate ourselves from these wondrous contradictions in light of this strange claim, except by judging it as false, not built on a basis that proves its truth, but rather of what their deceitful minds fabricated; to promote their merchandise which without such would not find a way to circulation. But God perfects His light, even if the obfuscators dislike it.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of a claim that certain Indian scholars (like al-Sirhindi and Shah Wali Allah) rejected rational sciences such as kalam and logic. The author points out a glaring contradiction: these scholars are said to have detested these sciences and focused only on hadith, yet they left behind numerous theological and logical works. Moreover, they held in high esteem scholars like al-Bihari and Bahr al-Ulum, who are known for their contributions to rational sciences. The author then argues that the only way to resolve this contradiction is to conclude that the claim is false—it is a fabrication invented to promote their own agenda. The passage ends with a Quranic allusion, affirming that truth prevails despite the efforts of those who mix truth with falsehood.

Key Terms
علم الكلام
الكتب الكلامية والمنطقية

D) Their praise of Shah Wali Allah.

Key Takeaway
A claim is disproven when it is contradicted by concrete evidence, such as the scholarly works of those who allegedly rejected a field.

Why Knowledge Cannot Be Entirely A Priori or A Posteriori
Author proves knowledge is neither purely intuitive nor purely discursive

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the compound argument proving that knowledge must include both intuitive and acquired elements, and identify the fallacies of circularity and infinite regress.

Core Idea
Knowledge is a mix: pure intuition is impossible, pure acquisition leads to circularity or infinite regress.

This passage tackles a foundational question in Islamic epistemology: can all our knowledge be either entirely self-evident (badihi) or entirely acquired through reflection (nazari)? The author argues no, using a sophisticated compound argument that combines a reductio ad absurdum with a mathematical refutation of infinite regress. Let's walk through the logic step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the thesis being refuted
The author targets the claim that either all knowledge is a priori (badihi) or all a posteriori (nazari). Locate where he states 'Not all of each of them is a priori... nor all a posteriori'.
The thesis is extreme: total uniformity of knowledge type.
2
Examine the consequence of universal a priori
If all knowledge were a priori, we would be self-sufficient and need no reflection. But we know reflection (nazar) is necessary for many truths. Therefore, universal a priori is false.
This is a modus tollens: if P then Q, not Q, so not P.
3
Examine the consequence of universal a posteriori
If all knowledge were acquired, each piece would depend on prior knowledge, leading to circularity (dawr) or infinite regress (tasalsul). The author claims both are impossible.
Circularity makes a thing precede itself; regress goes on forever.
4
Analyze the refutation of infinite regress
The author uses a doubling (taf‘if) argument: if the chain of dependencies were infinite, doubling it would yield a larger infinite, which is contradictory because the 'increased-upon' cannot be infinite. Thus, the chain must be finite.
This argument shows that an actual infinite series cannot be extended.
5
Synthesize the conclusion
Since neither extreme works, each of tasawwur and tasdiq must contain both a priori and a posteriori parts. The simple (basit) cannot be acquired, so complex knowledge requires arrangement via nazar and fikr.
The conclusion is a balanced epistemology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَيْسَ الْكُلُّ مِنْ كُلِّ مِنْهُمَا بَدِيهِيَّا، وَإِلَّا فَأَنْتَ مُسْتَغْنٍ، وَلَا نَظْرِيًّا ، مُتَوَفِّفًا عَلَى النَّظرِ ، وَإِلَّا لَدَارَ ، فَيَلْزَمُ تَقَدُّمُ الشَّيْءٍ عَلَى نَفْسِهِ بِمَرْتَبَتَيْنِ ، بَلْ بِمَرَاتِبَ غَيْرِ مُتَنَاهِيَة، فَإِنَّ الدَّوْرَ مُسْتَلْزِمٌ لِلتَّسَلْسُل ، أَوْ تَسَلْسَلَ، وَهُوَ بَاطِلٌ ; لِأَنَّ عَدَدَ التَّفْعِيفِ أَزْيَدُ مِنْ عَدَدِ الْأَصْلِ، وَكُلُّ عَدَدَيْنِ أَحَدُهُمَا أَزْيَدُ مِنَ الْآخَرِ فَزِيَادَةُ الزَّائِدِ بَعْدَ انْصِرَامِ جَمِيعِ أَحَادِ الْمَزِيدِ عَلَيْهِ; فَإِنَّ الْمَبْدَأَ لَا يُتَصَوَّرُ عَلَيْهِ الزَّيَادَةُ ، وَالْأَوْسَاطُ مُنتَظِمَةٌ مُتَوَالِيَةٌ ، فَحِينَئِذٍ لَوْ كَانَ الْمَزِيدُ عَلَيْهِ غَيْرَ مُتَنَاهِ لَزِمَ الزِّيَادَةُ فِي جَانِبِ عَدَم التناهي، وَهُوَ بَاطِلٌ، وَتَنَاهِي الْعَدَدِ يَسْتَلْزِمُ تَنَاهِيَ الْمَعْدُودِ، فَتَدَبَّر. وَلَا يُعْلَمُ التَّصَوُّرُ مِنَ التَّصْدِيقِ وَبِالْعَكْس ، لأَنَّ الْمُعَرَّفَ مَقُولٌ، وَالتَّصَوُّرُ مُتَسَاوِي النَّسْبَةِ، فَبَعْضُ كُلِّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا بَدِيهِيٌّ ، وَبَعْضُهُ نَظرِي، وَالْبَسِيطُ لَا يَكُونُ كَاسِبًا ، فَلا بُدَّ مِنْ تَرْتِيبٍ أُمُورٍ لِلاكْتِسَابِ، وَهُوَ النَّظْرُ وَالْفِكْرُ.
Not all of each of them is a priori; otherwise you would be self-sufficient. Nor all a posteriori, depending on reflection; otherwise there would be circularity, and then precedence of a thing before itself by two degrees, indeed by infinite degrees. For circularity necessitates regress, or it regresses infinitely, and that is false. Because the number of doubling is greater than the number of the original, and for any two numbers where one is greater than the other, the increase of the greater occurs after the exhaustion of all units of the lesser; for the beginning cannot be conceived to have increase, and the intermediates are consecutive and ordered. Then if the increased-upon were infinite, there would be increase in the direction of infinity, which is false. And the finitude of number necessitates the finitude of the numbered. So reflect. And tasawwur is not known from tasdiq nor vice versa, because the defined is predicated, and tasawwur is equally related. So part of each of them is a priori and part a posteriori. The simple cannot be acquired, so there must be an arrangement of things for acquisition, which is reflection (nazar) and thought (fikr).
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage proves that knowledge cannot be entirely a priori (badihi) or entirely a posteriori (nadari). If all knowledge were a priori, we would need no reflection; if all were a posteriori, we would need prior knowledge leading to circularity or infinite regress. The author demonstrates the impossibility of infinite regress using a numerical argument: doubling (taf‘if) yields a number greater than the original; if the original is infinite, the doubled number would also be infinite but with added units, which is contradictory. Therefore, the series must be finite. Consequently, each of tasawwur and tasdiq contains both a priori and a posteriori parts. The simple (basit) cannot be acquired, so acquisition requires arrangement of concepts through nazar and fikr.

Key Terms
بديهي
نظري
دور
تسلسل
تفعيف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the author argue would happen if all knowledge were a posteriori?
A) We would be self-sufficient.
B) We would need no reflection.
C) There would be circularity or infinite regress.
D) Tasawwur would be known from tasdiq.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge must be a combination of intuitive and acquired components; attempting to reduce it entirely to either leads to logical impossibilities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Using the same compound argument structure, show that not all principles of tafsir are either self-evident (badihi) or purely interpretive (nazari). Explicitly state the impossibility of circularity and infinite regress in the chain of interpretive authority.

The Famous Doubt about Differentia and Its Solutions
Author presents two objections against the simplicity of differentia and provides their resolution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the two famous objections against the simplicity and uniqueness of the differentia and explain how each is resolved by appeal to the simplicity of the differentia and the nature of composition.

Core Idea
The differentia is simple: no infinite regress and no multiplicity can threaten its uniqueness.

The differentia (fasl) is the core of definition, but two powerful objections challenge its simplicity. The first, cited from Avicenna's al-Shifa, argues that every differentia would require another differentia, leading to infinite regress. The second, an original objection from the author, argues that a universal like 'animal' applies to multiple species, each with its own differentia, so the composite would have multiple differentiae. The author resolves both by insisting on the simplicity of the differentia and clarifying the nature of composition. By understanding these objections, you will sharpen your grasp of how definitions are constructed and defended.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first objection (from al-Shifa) and its regress argument
The objection claims that every differentia is a concept; if it is not the most universal, it must be separated from its associates by another differentia, leading to an infinite chain. Find this reasoning in the Arabic: 'فَإِمَّا أَعَمُّ الْمَحْمُولَاتِ أَوْ تَحْتَهُ، وَالْأَوَّلُ بَاطِلٌ، فَهُوَ مُنْفَصِلٌ عَنِ الْمُشَارِكَاتِ بِفَصْلٍ، فَإِذَنْ لِكُلِّ فَصْلٍ فَصْلٌ، وَيَتَسَلْسَلُ.'
The term 'tasalsul' (infinite regress) is key.
2
Analyze the solution to the first objection
The author rejects the assumption that every concept must be distinguished by a differentia. Only if the universal is constitutive (muqawwim) of the concept would a differentia be needed. The differentia itself is simple (basit), so it cannot be broken down into further constituents. Find: 'أَنَّا لَا نُسَلِّمُ انْفِصَالَ كُلِّ مَفْهُومٍ بِالْفَصْلِ... فَإِنَّ الْفَصْلَ بَسِيطٌ.'
Simplicity blocks the regress.
3
Identify the second objection (the author's own) about multiple differentiae
The universal applies to many individuals with one truth. For example, 'animal' applies to both human and horse. The composite of human and horse is animal, but human and horse have different near differentiae (rational and neighing). So it seems the composite has two differentiae, which is absurd. Find: 'فَمَجْمُوعُ الْإِنْسَانِ وَالْفَرَسِ حَيَوَانٌ، فَلَهُ فَصْلَانِ قَرِيبَانِ.'
This challenges the uniqueness of the differentia for a universal.
4
Examine the solution to the second objection
The composite is one effect of multiple causes; multiplicity of causal aspects does not entail real multiplicity in the effect. The differentia of the composite is one simple concept. Find: 'لِأَنَّهُ مَعْلُولٌ وَاحِدٌ وَعِلَّةٌ كَثِيرَةٌ، وَكَثْرَةُ جِهَاتِ الْمَعْلُولِيَّةِ لَا تَسْتَلْزِمُ كَثْرَةَ الْمَعْلُولِيَّةِ حَقِيقِيَّةً.'
The composite's differentia is simple despite multiple causes.
5
Handle the sub-objection about impossible composites (partners of God)
A further objection: if the sum of two impossible beings (e.g., partners of God) is also impossible, but composites are possible, contradiction arises. The solution: the sum is one entity, and further composites from it are merely accidental (i'tibari), not real, so no infinite regress. Find: 'الرَّابِعُ أَمْرٌ اعْتِبَارِيٌّ... وَالتَّسَلْسُلُ فِي الِاعْتِبَارِيَّاتِ مُنْقَطِعٌ.'
Accidental considerations do not generate real infinite regress.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَهَهُنَا شَكٍّ مَشْهُورٌ مِنْ وَجْهَيْنِ ، الْأَوَّلُ : مَا أَوْرَدَهُ فِي الشَّفَاءِ»، وَهُوَ أَنَّ كُلَّ فَصْلٍ مَعْنى مِنَ الْمَعَانِي، فَإِمَّا أَعَمُّ الْمَحْمُولَاتِ أَوْ تَحْتَهُ، وَالْأَوَّلُ بَاطِلٌ، فَهُوَ مُنْفَصِلٌ عَنِ الْمُشَارِكَاتِ بِفَصْلٍ، فَإِذَنْ لِكُلِّ فَصْلٍ فَصْلٌ، وَيَتَسَلْسَلُ. وَحَلَّهُ: أَنَّا لَا نُسَلِّمُ انْفِصَالَ كُلِّ مَفْهُومٍ بِالْفَصْلِ، وَإِنَّمَا يَجِبُ لَوْ كَانَ ذَلِكَ الْعَامُّ مُقَوِّمًا لَهُ وَذَا مَمْنُوعٌ؛ فَإِنَّ الْفَصْلَ بَسِيطٌ. وَالثَّاني: مَا سَنَحَ لِي، وَهُوَ أَنَّ الْكُلِّيَّ كَمَا يَصْدُقُ عَلَى وَاحِدٍ مِنْ أَفْرَادِهِ يَصْدُقُ عَلَى كَثِيرِينَ مِنْ أَفْرَادِهِ بِصِدْقٍ وَاحِدٍ، فَمَجْمُوعُ الْإِنْسَانِ وَالْفَرَسِ حَيَوَانٌ، فَلَهُ فَصْلَانِ قَرِيبَانِ. لَا يُقَالُ: يَلْزَمُ صِدْقُ الْعِلَّةِ عَلَى الْمَعْلُولِ الْمُرَكَّبِ؛ لِأَنَّهُ مَجْمُوعُ الْمَادِّيَّةِ وَالصُّورِيَّةِ، وَهُوَ مُحَالٌ، وَذَلِكَ لِأَنَّ الِاسْتِحَالَةَ مَمْنُوعَةٌ؛ فَإِنَّهُ مَعْلُولٌ وَاحِدٌ وَعِلَّةٌ كَثِيرَةٌ، وَكَثْرَةُ جِهَاتِ الْمَعْلُولِيَّةِ لَا تَسْتَلْزِمُ كَثْرَةَ الْمَعْلُولِيَّةِ حَقِيقِيَّةً. لَا يُقَالُ: فَمَجْمُوعُ شَرِيكَيْ الْبَارِي شَرِيكُ الْبَارِي، فَبَعْضُ شَرِيكِ الْبَارِي مُرَكَّبٌ، وَكُلُّ مُرَكَّبٍ مُمْكِنٌ، فَبَعْضُ شَرِيكِ الْبَارِي مُمْكِنٌ، مَعَ أَنَّ كُلَّ شَرِيكِ الْبَارِي مُمْتَنِعٌ؛ لِأَنَّ إِمْكَانَ كُلِّ مُرَكَّبٍ مَمْنُوعٌ؛ فَإِنَّ افْتِقَارَ الِاجْتِمَاعِ عَلَى تَقْدِيرِ الْوُجُودِ الْفَرْضِيِّ لَا يَضُرُّ الِامْتِنَاعَ فِي نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ. أَلَا تَرَى أَنَّهُ يَسْتَلْزِمُ الْمُحَالَ بِالذَّاتِ، فَلَا يَكُونُ مُمْكِنًا، فَتَدَبَّرْ. وَحَلُّهُ: أَنَّ وُجُودَ اثْنَيْنِ يَسْتَلْزِمُ وُجُودَ ثَالِثٍ، وَهُوَ الْمَجْمُوعُ، وَذَلِكَ وَاحِدٌ. لَا يُقَالُ: عَلَى هَذَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ تَحَقُّقِ اثْنَيْنِ تَحَقُّقُ أُمُورٍ غَيْرِ مُتَنَاهِيَةٍ؛ لِأَنَّهُ بِضَمِّ الثَّالِثِ يَتَحَقَّقُ الرَّابِعُ وَهَكَذَا؛ لِأَنَّا نَقُولُ: الرَّابِعُ أَمْرٌ اعْتِبَارِيٌّ؛ فَإِنَّهُ حَصَلَ بِاعْتِبَارِ شَيْءٍ وَاحِدٍ مَرَّتَيْنِ، وَالتَّسَلْسُلُ فِي الِاعْتِبَارِيَّاتِ مُنْقَطِعٌ بِانْقِطَاعِهِ، فَافْهَمْ.
And here there is a famous doubt from two aspects. The first: what was cited in al-Shifa, namely that every differentia is a concept among concepts; it is either the most universal of predicates or under it; the first is false, so it is separated from its associates by a differentia; then every differentia has a differentia, leading to infinite regress. Its solution: we do not accept that every concept is separated by a differentia; rather, it would only be necessary if that universal were constitutive of it, and that is not granted, for the differentia is simple. The second: what occurred to me, namely that the universal, just as it applies to one of its individuals, applies to many of its individuals with one truth; thus the sum of human and horse is an animal, so it has two near differentiae. It cannot be said: this implies the truth of the cause of the composite effect, because it is the composite of matter and form, and that is absurd; but that absurdity is rejected, for it is one effect and many causes, and multiplicity of aspects of effectuality does not entail a real multiplicity of effectuality. It cannot be said: then the sum of two partners of God is a partner of God, so some partner of God is composite, and every composite is possible, so some partner of God is possible, although every partner of God is impossible; because the possibility of every composite is denied; for the need for composition on the supposition of hypothetical existence does not harm impossibility in the real state. Do you not see that it entails the impossible by essence, so it cannot be possible? So ponder. Its solution: the existence of two entails the existence of a third, which is the sum, and that is one. It cannot be said: on this, the realization of two entails the realization of infinite things, because by adding the third, the fourth is realized, and so on; because we say: the fourth is an accidental affair, for it arises by considering one thing twice, and infinite regress in accidental matters is cut off by its termination, so understand.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage presents two famous objections (shakk) regarding the differentia, followed by their solutions. The first objection, from Avicenna's al-Shifa, argues that every differentia is a concept: if it is not the most universal, it must be distinguished from its associates by another differentia, leading to an infinite regress. The solution denies the assumption that every concept must be separated by a differentia; the differentia is simple (basit), and only a constitutive universal would require such separation, which does not apply. The second objection is the author's own: a universal like 'animal' applies truthfully to both 'human' and 'horse' as individuals, so the composite 'human+horse' is animal; since human and horse have different differentiae (rational and neighing), this composite would have two near differentiae, which is impossible. The objection is rebutted by noting that the composite is a single effect of multiple causes; multiplicity of causal aspects does not entail real multiplicity. Then a further objection about partners of God (impossible beings) is raised: if the sum of two impossible beings were also impossible, but since composites are possible, a contradiction arises. The solution clarifies that the sum of two things is one entity, and any further composites from that are merely accidental (i'tibari), not real, thus no infinite regress. This strengthens the doctrine that differentia is simple and unique.

Key Terms
شَكّ (shakk): doubt or objection
حَلّ (hall): solution
تَسَلْسُل (tasalsul): infinite regress
بَسِيط (basit): simple

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the solution to the first doubt (from al-Shifa)?
A) Every differentia is a genus for a lower differentia.
B) The differentia is composite and can be defined.
C) We do not accept that every concept must be distinguished by a differentia, because the differentia is simple.
D) Infinite regress is allowed.

Key Takeaway
The differentia is simple (basit), so it neither requires another differentia (avoiding regress) nor admits multiplicity even when applied to composites.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct the first objection (infinite regress) in your own words using a definition from fiqh: e.g., define 'salah' and then attack its differentia as requiring further differentiae.
?
Apply the solution to the second objection to the definition of 'sawm' (fasting): if 'sawm' is defined as 'abstention from food, drink, and sexual intercourse', how would you defend its differentia as simple using the author's reasoning about composite effects?

The Harmlessness of Infinite Regress in Mental Constructs
Author raises and solves a doubt about the necessary relation itself leading to infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why infinite regress of necessary relations is not problematic by distinguishing mental-intentional meanings from real entities.

Core Idea
Infinite regress is only vicious for external realities; mental constructs stop when consideration ends.

The passage tackles a subtle doubt: if a necessary relation itself is necessary, we get an infinite regress. The author solves it by showing that such relations are purely mental, so the regress is harmless. This is key to understanding the epistemology of logical relations and avoiding false objections in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the doubt
The doubt is that the relation of necessity (luzum) must itself be a necessary property, otherwise the original relation collapses. This implies an infinite chain of necessary relations.
This is a classic regress argument applied to logical relations.
2
Locate the solution
The author states that luzum is a 'ma'na i'tibari intiza'i' – a meaning that exists only in the mind after an act of consideration.
Such meanings have no external reality independent of mental consideration.
3
Understand the implication
Because the regression exists only within mental consideration, it stops when consideration stops. There is no infinite regress of real entities.
The chain is not a regress of things in the world, but of mental abstractions.
4
Distinguish from external realities
The source (mansha') of the mental construct is real, but the construct itself is not independently existent. This preserves objectivity without requiring infinite regress in the real world.
In Kalam, for example, the regress of causes is impossible because causes are real; here the regress is only mental.
5
Apply to avoid fallacies
Recognise that not all infinite regresses are logically impossible; those among mental abstractions are acceptable. This prevents false objections against arguments that involve such relations.
For instance, in definitions and classifications, recursive definitions may involve mental regress that is harmless.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَهُنَا شَكٍّ ، وَهُوَ : أَنَّ اللُّزُومَ لَازمٌ ، وَإِلَّا يَنْهَدِمُ أَصْلُ الْمُلَازَمَةِ ، فَيَتَسَلْسَلُ اللُّزُومَاتُ . وَحَلَّهُ: أَنَّ اللُّزُومَ مِنَ الْمَعَانِي الْإِعْتِبَارِيَّةِ الاِنْتِزَاعِيَّةِ، الَّتِي لَيْسَ لَهَا تَحَقُّق إِلَّا في الذِّهْنِ بَعْدَ اعْتِبَارِهِ، فَيَنْقَطِعُ بِانْقِطَاعِ الاِعْتِبَارِ. نَعَمْ، مَنْشَؤُهَا وَمَنْبَعُهَا مُتَحَقِّقُ ، وَذَلِكَ هُوَ الْحَافِظُ لِنَفْس الْأَمْرِيَّةِ  لِلاِنْتِزَاعِيَّاتِ، مُتَنَاهِيَةً أَوْ غَيْرَ مُتَنَاهِيَةٍ، مُرَتَّبَةً أَوْ غَيْرَ مُرَتَّبَةٍ ، فَقَوْلُهُمْ: التَّسَلْسُلُ فِيهَا لَيْسَ بِمُحَالٍ صَادِقٌ بِعَدَمِ الْمَوْضُوعِ ، فتدبر .
And here is a doubt: that the necessary relation [luzum] is itself a necessary property, otherwise the basis of the relation of necessity would collapse, and so there would be an infinite regress of necessary relations. Its solution: that the necessary relation is among the mental-intentional [i'tibari intiza'i] meanings, which have no reality except in the mind after considering them, so it stops with the cessation of the consideration. Yes, its origin and source are real, and that is what preserves the objectivity [nafs al-amriyya] of the mental constructs, whether finite or infinite, ordered or unordered. So their statement: 'infinite regression in them is not impossible' is true due to the non-existence of the subject, so ponder.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion of a doubt concerning the concept of necessary relation (luzum). The doubt is: if we say that A necessarily entails B, then the relation of necessity itself is a property that must also be necessary to avoid collapse, leading to an infinite chain of necessary relations. The solution: luzum is an i'tibari intiza'i meaning (a mentally-considered abstract concept) that only exists in the mind after an act of consideration. When we stop considering, the chain stops. This does not undermine objectivity because the source (origin) of these mental constructs is real. Infinite regress in such mental considerations is not impossible because the subject (the relation itself) is not real in an external sense. The author uses the example of mental concepts in logic, such as the relation between a subject and its predicate in a categorical proposition. The key takeaway is that not all infinite regresses are vicious; those among mental considerations are harmless as they depend on our cognitive act.

Key Terms
لزوم (luzum): the relation of necessary consequence
معنى اعتباري انتزاعي (ma'na i'tibari intiza'i): a mental-intentional meaning
تسلسل (tasalsul): infinite regress

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that infinite regress of necessary relations is not problematic?
A) Because the relation of necessity is a real entity in the external world.
B) Because the relation is a mental construct that ceases when consideration stops.
C) Because each necessary relation is different from the previous one.
D) Because infinite regress is impossible regardless of the nature of the entities.

Key Takeaway
Infinite regress in mental-intentional meanings is harmless because such meanings have no extramental reality and cease when consideration ends.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Resolving the Infinite Regress Objection for Luzum
Author resolves the doubt that luzum itself leads to infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the infinite regress objection against the concept of necessity (luzum) and explain the author's solution using the distinction between mental i'tibari relations and extra-mental reality.

Core Idea
Infinite regress is only problematic for extra-mental entities; mental i'tibari relations do not cause an infinite chain.

In this passage, the author tackles a subtle doubt: if 'luzum' (necessary relation) is itself a relation, wouldn't it require another relation to connect it, leading to an infinite regress? This objection threatens the very foundation of definitional logic. The author's solution is elegant: luzum is an i'tibari concept, existing only in the mind upon consideration. By tracing the chain back to its mental source, the regress is halted.

Guided Path
1
Identify the doubt (shakk)
Locate the objection: 'al-luzumu lazimum' — the relation itself is a necessary relation. If not, the original correlation would collapse. This implies an infinite chain of relations.
The doubt is that every relation requires another relation ad infinitum.
2
Analyze the solution's core claim
The author states that luzum is among 'al-ma'ani al-i'tibariyya al-intiza'iyya' (mental relational concepts). These have no external existence; they exist only in the mind after consideration.
The key is the mental vs. extra-mental distinction.
3
Understand how the regress stops
Since i'tibari concepts cease when consideration ceases, the chain of relations is not infinite in extra-mental reality. The regress is only a mental series that can be terminated by stopping the consideration.
Infinity in mental concepts is not impossible because there is no actual infinite extra-mental subject.
4
Distinguish between source and relation
The author notes that the source (mansha') of these relations is real and preserves factuality, but the relations themselves are mental. This preserves objectivity without requiring an infinite extra-mental chain.
The source is like the foundation; the relations are the mental scaffolding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يُقَالُ عَلَى الَّذِي يَلْزَمُ مِنْ تَصَوُّرِهِمَا الْجَزْمُ بِاللُّزُومِ، وَهُوَ أَعَمُّ مِنَ الْأَوَّلِ. أَوْ غَيْرُ بَيْنِ بِخِلَافِهِ، فَالنِّسْبَةُ بِالْعَكْسِ، وَكُلُّ مِنْهُمَا مَوْجُودٌ بِالضَّرُورَةِ. وَهَهُنَا شَكٍّ ، وَهُوَ : أَنَّ اللُّزُومَ لَازمٌ ، وَإِلَّا يَنْهَدِمُ أَصْلُ الْمُلَازَمَةِ ، فَيَتَسَلْسَلُ اللُّزُومَاتُ . وَحَلَّهُ: أَنَّ اللُّزُومَ مِنَ الْمَعَانِي الْإِعْتِبَارِيَّةِ الاِنْتِزَاعِيَّةِ، الَّتِي لَيْسَ لَهَا تَحَقُّق إِلَّا في الذِّهْنِ بَعْدَ اعْتِبَارِهِ، فَيَنْقَطِعُ بِانْقِطَاعِ الاِعْتِبَارِ. نَعَمْ، مَنْشَؤُهَا وَمَنْبَعُهَا مُتَحَقِّقُ ، وَذَلِكَ هُوَ الْحَافِظُ لِنَفْس الْأَمْرِيَّةِ  ِللاِنْتِزَاعِيَّاتِ، مُتَنَاهِيَةً أَوْ غَيْرَ مُتَنَاهِيَةٍ، مُرَتَّبَةً أَوْ غَيْرَ مُرَتَّبَةٍ ، فَقَوْلُهُمْ: التَّسَلْسُلُ فِيهَا لَيْسَ بِمُحَالٍ صَادِقٌ بِعَدَمِ الْمَوْضُوعِ ، فتدر .
It is said about that from which conceiving both results in certainty of the relation, and it is more general than the first. Or it is not evident contrary to it, so the relation is reversed, and each of them exists necessarily. And here there is a doubt: that the relation (luzum) is itself a necessary relation, otherwise the basis of correlation would collapse, so relations would regress infinitely. And its solution: that the relation is among the mental relational concepts, which have no realization except in the mind after its consideration, so it ceases with the cessation of consideration. Yes, their source and origin is realized, and that is what preserves the very factuality for the relational concepts, whether finite or infinite, ordered or unordered. So their saying: 'Regression in them is not impossible' is true due to the absence of the subject. So reflect.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage is a critical discussion of a potential infinite regress objection regarding the nature of necessity (luzum). The author first clarifies the types of necessity mentioned earlier, then presents the doubt: if luzum itself is a necessary relation, then it would require another relation to connect it, leading to an infinite chain. The solution is that luzum is an i'tibari (relational) concept that has no external existence; it only exists in the mind after consideration. Therefore, when consideration stops, the chain stops. The author notes that the source of these relations is real, but the relations themselves are mental constructs, so infinite regression is not impossible because there is no actual infinite chain of extra-mental entities.

اللُّزُوم

Key Takeaway
Necessary relations (luzum) are mental i'tibari concepts; their regress is harmless because it terminates with the cessation of mental consideration, preserving the validity of definitions.

The Natural Universal and Its External Existence
Author presents views on the natural universal's external existence and refutes the simple essence view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of universals and explain why the natural universal exists externally only through individuals, using the author's refutation of the simple essence view.

Core Idea
The natural universal exists in individuals, not separately; the simple essence view leads to contradiction.

This passage tackles a foundational debate in Islamic ontology: do universals exist outside the mind? The author first clarifies that only the natural universal is a candidate, then defends the position that it exists through individuals, and finally refutes the alternative claim that only simple essences exist.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of universals
Locate the three types: logical, rational, and natural. Note that the logical universal is a secondary intelligible with no external existence, and the rational universal likewise does not exist externally. Only the natural universal is disputed.
The author states that no one holds the logical universal to exist externally.
2
Understand the muhaqqiqun's position
The verifiers (muhaqqiqun), including Ibn Sina, hold that the natural universal exists externally in the very existence of individuals. Existence is one in essence, but the existent is two: the individual and the universal, with the universal being an accident to the individual in terms of unity.
Some verifiers also say the natural universal is sensible in a general way—a position the author endorses as true.
3
Examine the minority view
A small group of philosophers assert that the existent is the simple essence (al-huwiyya al-basita) and that universals are merely mental abstractions. This view denies any composition in individuals.
This view is the target of the author's critique.
4
Analyze the author's reductio
The author uses Zayd as an example: if Zayd is a simple essence in every respect, with no relations even to existence or non-existence, then it is impossible to abstract multiple distinct forms from him. The proponents would have to claim that the simple has two distinct yet corresponding forms, which is contradictory.
This demonstrates the absurdity of the simple essence position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعْلَمْ أَنَّ الْمَنْطِقِيَّ مِنَ الْمَعْقُولَاتِ الثَّانِيَةِ، وَمِنْ ثَمَّ لَمْ يَذْهَبْ أَحَدٌ إِلَى وُجُودِهِ فِي الْخَارِج وَإِذَا لَمْ يَكُنِ الْمَنْطِقِيُّ مَوْجُودًا لَمْ يَكُنِ الْعَقْلِيُّ مَوْجُودًا . . بَقِيَ الطَّبَعِيُّ ، اخْتُلِفَ فِيهِ، فَمَذْهَبُ المُحَقِّقِينَ - وَمِنْهُمْ الشَّيْخُ الرَّئيسُ - أَنَّهُ مَوْجُودٌ فِي الْخَارِج بِعَيْنِ وُجُودِ الْأَفْرَادِ ، فَالْوُجُودُ وَاحِدٌ بِالذَّاتِ، وَالْمَوْجُودُ اثْنَانِ ، وَهُوَ عَارِض لَهُمَا ، مِنْ حَيْثُ الْوَحْدَةُ، وَمَنْ ذَهَبَ مِنْهُمْ إِلَى عَدَمِيَّةِ التَّعَيُّن قَالَ بِمَحْسُوسِيَّتِهِ أَيْضًا فِي الْجُمْلَةِ، وَهُوَ الْحَقُّ. وَذَهَبَ شِرْدِمَةٌ قَلِيلَةٌ مِنَ الْمُتَفَلْسِفِينَ إِلَى أَنَّ الْمَوْجُودَ هُوَ الْهُويَّةُ البَسِيطَةُ، أَوِ الْكُلُّباتُ مُنتَزَعَاتٌ عَقْلِيَّةٌ ، وَلَيْتَ شِعْرِي إِذَا كَانَ زَيْدٌ مَثَلًا بَسِيطًا مِنْ كُلِّ وَجْهِ، وَلُوحِظَ إِلَيْهِ مِنْ حَيْثُ هُوَ هُوَ ، مِنْ غَيْرِ نَفْرٍ إِلَى مُشَارِكَاتٍ وَمُبَايِنَاتٍ، حَتَّى عَنِ الْوُجُودِ وَالْعَدَم ، كَيْفَ يُتَصَوَّرُ مِنْهُ انْتِزَاعُ صُوَرٍ مُتَغَابِرَةٍ ، فَلَا بُدَّ لَهُمْ مِنَ الْقَوْلِ بِأَنَّ لِلْبَسِيطِ الْحَقِيقِيُّ فِي مَرْتَبَةِ تَقَوُّمِهِ وَتَحَصُّلِهِ صُورَتَيْنِ مُتَغَايِرَتَيْنِ مُطَابِقَتَيْنِ لَهُ ، وَهُوَ قَوْلُ بِالْمُتَنَافِيَيْنِ.
Know that the logical universal is among the secondary intelligibles, and therefore no one has held that it exists externally. And since the logical universal does not exist, the rational universal also does not exist. The natural universal remains; there is disagreement about it. The view of the verifiers (muhaqqiqun)—including the Chief Master (Ibn Sina)—is that it exists externally in the very existence of individuals, so existence is one in essence, while the existent is two, and it (the universal) is an accident to them with respect to unity. And those of them who adopted the non-existence of determination said that it is also sensible in general, and this is the truth. And a small group of philosophers went to the view that the existent is the simple essence, or that universals are mental abstractions. I wish I knew: if Zayd, for example, is simple in every respect, and he is considered as he is, without turning to commonalities and differences, even to existence and non-existence, how can multiple distinct forms be abstracted from him? So they must say that for the truly simple, in the level of its constitution and realization, there are two distinct forms that correspond to it, and this is saying two contradictory things.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological and critical discussion about the existence of the natural universal in the external world. The author first establishes that the logical universal (the concept) is a secondary intelligible and does not exist externally, and consequently the rational universal (which combines logical and natural) also does not exist externally. Only the natural universal is a candidate for external existence. The author presents the view of the muhaqqiqun (including Ibn Sina) that the natural universal exists externally through the very existence of its individuals: existence is one (the individual's existence) but the existent is two (the individual and the universal), with the universal being an accident to the individual in terms of unity. Some (like Fakhr al-Din al-Razi?) argued for the non-existence of determination, claiming the natural universal is sensible in a general way, which the author endorses as correct. Then the author criticizes a minority of philosophers who hold that the existent is a simple essence (the individual stripped of all attributes) and that universals are merely mental constructs. The author argues by example: if Zayd is considered as a pure simple essence without any relations, it is impossible to abstract multiple distinct forms from him. Thus, these philosophers would have to claim that the truly simple has two distinct yet corresponding forms, which is contradictory. The author is thus defending the reality of the natural universal at the level of individuals.

Key Terms
المَعْقُولَات الثَّانِيَة
الْمُحَقِّقُونَ
الْهُويَّةُ البَسِيطَةُ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the view of the muhaqqiqun (verifiers) regarding the natural universal?
A) It exists externally as a separate entity.
B) It exists externally in the very existence of its individuals.
C) It exists only in the mind.
D) It is a simple essence with no attributes.

Key Takeaway
The natural universal exists only through the individuals; the simple essence view is self-contradictory because a truly simple thing cannot yield multiple distinct forms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Some philosophers argue that the universal 'human' exists externally as a separate Platonic form. Using the muhaqqiqun's position from this passage, explain why this is incorrect and how the natural universal exists instead.
?
Suppose a student claims that Zayd is a simple essence with no attributes. Following the author's reductio, show why this claim leads to a contradiction by considering how we can predicate multiple attributes (like 'rational', 'animal') of Zayd.

Existence of Natural, Logical, and Mental Universals
Author discusses existence of three types of universal: natural, logical, and mental.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between natural, logical, and mental universals and critique the view that all universals are mere mental abstractions using a reductio argument.

Core Idea
The natural universal exists in external individuals; logical and mental universals are in the mind.

Universals are central to Islamic logic and metaphysics. The author of the Sullam al-Ulum explains three types: the natural universal (existing in the world), the logical universal (concept in the mind), and the mental universal (abstracted form). He defends the realist position that the natural universal has extra-mental existence and critiques the view that reduces universals to mental constructs. Understanding these distinctions is key to debates in kalam and usul al-fiqh over the reality of attributes and categories.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of universals
Locate the terms 'الطبيعي', 'المنطقي', and 'العقلي' in the passage. Note that the logical universal is the concept predicable of many, the mental universal is the abstract form, and the natural universal is the one existing in external individuals.
The natural universal is also called the 'mixed' or 'absolute' universal when considered with or without particularities.
2
Understand the dependency between logical and mental universals
The author states: 'when the logical does not exist, the mental does not exist.' This means the concept (logical) must first be in the mind before an abstracted form (mental) can be considered.
This establishes a priority: the logical universal is the immediate object of predication.
3
Analyze the verifiers' position on the natural universal
The author endorses the view that the natural universal exists 'with the very existence of the individuals' (بِعَيْنِ وُجُودِ الْأَفْرَادِ). Existence is one, but the existent is two: the individual and its universal nature.
The universal is an accident in terms of unity, i.e., it is one in many.
4
Examine the reductio argument against the simple identity view
The author critiques those who claim existence is simple identity or universals are mere mental abstractions. Using Zayd as an example: if Zayd is simple in every aspect, how can multiple diverse forms be abstracted from him? That would require two contradictory forms for the same simple thing.
This is a conditional argument: if Zayd is simple, then abstraction of multiple forms is impossible; but abstraction occurs, so Zayd is not simple.
5
Distinguish abstract universals and Plato's error
The author notes that no one posits abstract universals (separate from matter and mind) except Plato with his forms, which is criticized. Abstract universals do exist in the mind, however, as 'there is no restriction on conceptualizations.'
The abstract universal here means something like the Platonic Form, not the mental concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
متن سلم العلوم
وُجُودِهِ فِي الْخَارِج وَإِذَا لَمْ يَكُنِ الْمَنْطِقِيُّ مَوْجُودًا لَمْ يَكُنِ الْعَقْلِيُّ مَوْجُودًا . . بَقِيَ الطَّبَعِيُّ ، اخْتُلِفَ فِيهِ، فَمَذْهَبُ المُحَقِّقِينَ - وَمِنْهُمْ الشَّيْخُ الرَّئيسُ - أَنَّهُ مَوْجُودٌ فِي الْخَارِج بِعَيْنِ وُجُودِ الْأَفْرَادِ ، فَالْوُجُودُ وَاحِدٌ بِالذَّاتِ، وَالْمَوْجُودُ اثْنَانِ ، وَهُوَ عَارِض لَهُمَا ، مِنْ حَيْثُ الْوَحْدَةُ، وَمَنْ ذَهَبَ مِنْهُمْ إِلَى عَدَمِيَّةِ التَّعَيُّن قَالَ بِمَحْسُوسِيَّتِهِ أَيْضًا فِي الْجُمْلَةِ، وَهُوَ الْحَقُّ.
وَذَهَبَ شِرْدِمَةٌ قَلِيلَةٌ مِنَ الْمُتَفَلْسِفِينَ إِلَى أَنَّ الْمَوْجُودَ هُوَ الْهُويَّةُ البَسِيطَةُ، أَوِ الْكُلُّباتُ مُنتَزَعَاتٌ عَقْلِيَّةٌ ، وَلَيْتَ شِعْرِي إِذَا كَانَ زَيْدٌ مَثَلًا بَسِيطًا مِنْ كُلِّ وَجْهِ، وَلُوحِظَ إِلَيْهِ مِنْ حَيْثُ هُوَ هُوَ ، مِنْ غَيْرِ نَفْرٍ إِلَى مُشَارِكَاتٍ وَمُبَايِنَاتٍ، حَتَّى عَنِ الْوُجُودِ وَالْعَدَم ، كَيْفَ يُتَصَوَّرُ مِنْهُ انْتِزَاعُ صُوَرٍ مُتَغَابِرَةٍ ، فَلَا بُدَّ لَهُمْ مِنَ الْقَوْلِ بِأَنَّ لِلْبَسِيطِ الْحَقِيقِيُّ فِي مَرْتَبَةِ تَقَوُّمِهِ وَتَحَصُّلِهِ صُورَتَيْنِ مُتَغَايِرَتَيْنِ مُطَابِقَتَيْنِ لَهُ ، وَهُوَ قَوْلُ بِالْمُتَنَافِيَيْنِ.
هَذَا فِي الْمَخْلُوطَةِ وَالْمُطْلَقَةِ ، وَأَمَّا الْمُجَرَّدَةُ فَلَمْ يَذْهَبْ أَحَدٌ إِلَى وُجُودِهَا الْخَارِج ، إِلَّا أَفَلَاطُونُ ، وَهِيَ الْمُثْلُ الْأَفَلَا طُونِيَّةُ ، وَهَذَا مِمَّا يُشَنَّعُ بِهِ عَلَيْهِ. وَهَلْ تُوجَدُ الْمُجَرَّدَةُ فِي الذَّهْنِ ؟ قِيلَ لَا ، وَقِيلَ نَعَمْ، وَهُوَ الْحَقُّ؛ فَإِنَّهُ لَا حَجْرَ فِي التَّصَرُّرَاتِ .
Text of Sullam al-Ulum: ...its existence in the external. And when the logical [universal] does not exist, the mental [universal] does not exist. The natural [universal] remains, concerning which there is difference of opinion. The position of the verifiers – among them the Chief Master – is that it exists in the external with the very existence of the individuals. So existence is one in essence, and the existent is two, and it is an accident of them, in terms of unity. And whoever among them held the absence of determination said that it is also sensible in general, and that is the truth. And a small group of philosophers went to the view that the existent is the simple identity, or that universals are mental abstractions. Would that I knew: if Zayd, for example, is simple in every aspect, and is considered in terms of himself, without turning to commonalities and differences, even from existence and non-existence, how can one conceive of abstracting multiple diverse forms from him? They must necessarily say that the truly simple thing, at the level of its constitution and actualization, has two diverse forms corresponding to it, and that is a statement of two contradictories. This is regarding the mixed and the absolute. As for the abstract [universal], no one held its existence in the external except Plato – and they are the Platonic forms – and this is a criticism against him. And does the abstract exist in the mind? It is said: No, and it is said: Yes, and it is the truth, for there is no restriction on conceptualizations.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion on the existence of three types of universals: the natural (tabi'i), the logical (mantiqi), and the mental ('aqli). The author begins by establishing a dependency: if the logical universal (the concept in the mind) does not exist, the mental universal (the abstracted form) does not exist. The natural universal remains, and its external existence is debated. The verifiers, including Ibn Sina, hold that the natural universal exists in the external world with the same existence as the individual instances. Thus existence (wujud) is one in essence, but the existent (mawjud) is two: the individual and the universal, with the universal being an accident of the individuals in terms of unity. Those who deny determination (ta'ayyun) say the natural universal is also sensible in a general sense, and this is the truth. Next, the author critiques a small group of philosophers who reduce existence to simple identity or claim universals are mere mental abstractions. The reductio ad absurdum argument: if Zayd is simple from every aspect, considering him without regard to any commonalities or differences, how could multiple distinct forms be abstracted from him? That would require attributing two diverse forms to the same simple thing, which is contradictory. The author then distinguishes between mixed (mukhtalata) and absolute (mutlaqa) universals, which exist externally in this manner, and abstract (mujarrada) universals, which no one posits externally except Plato with his forms, which is criticized. Finally, the author asks whether abstract universals exist in the mind. Two opinions are cited: no and yes. The correct view is that they exist in the mind, since there is no restriction on conceptualizations.

Key Terms
الطبيعي
المنطقي
العقلي
المجردة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which type of universal exists in the external world with the same existence as the individuals?
A) Natural universal
B) Logical universal
C) Mental universal
D) Abstract universal

Key Takeaway
The natural universal has extra-mental existence; logical and mental universals are in the mind, and the view that denies external reality to universals leads to a logical contradiction.

Identify whether each of the following is a natural, logical, or mental universal: (a) the concept 'human' as used in a syllogism, (b) humanity considered as existing in all human individuals, (c) the abstract idea of 'humanity' stripped of all particularities.
?

The Composition Principle and Its Effect on Certainty
Author gives a concluding rule and states the components of sciences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle that a composition of a preponderant and a preponderated element is preponderated to evaluate mixed-premise arguments in Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
A chain is only as strong as its weakest link — and so is a logical composition.

In this brief concluding passage, the author presents a crucial logical rule: when you combine a certain (rajih) and a doubtful (marjūḥ) premise, the whole argument becomes doubtful. This principle is essential for maintaining rigor in theological and juristic reasoning. We will unpack this rule and see how it guards against building proofs on shaky foundations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle from the Arabic
Locate the sentence 'wa al-mu'allaf min al-rajih wa al-marjūḥ marjūḥ'. This is the core rule: the composition of the preponderant and the preponderated is itself preponderated.
The author adds 'so ponder' to stress careful reflection.
2
Understand the terms rajih and marjuh
Rajih means stronger, more likely true; marjuh means weaker, less likely true. In logic, these refer to the epistemic weight of propositions.
These terms are stable in Islamic logic and used in probabilist reasoning.
3
Apply the principle to a mixed-premise argument
Imagine a syllogism: one premise is certain (e.g., from Quran), the other is probable (e.g., from a single narration). According to this rule, the conclusion is only probable.
This prevents claiming certainty from mixed evidence.
4
Recognize why the rule matters for constructing proofs
In Islamic theology and fiqh, proofs must be based on certain premises (qat'i) to yield certain knowledge. Mixing certain and probable premises weakens the whole argument.
Scholars use this principle to judge the strength of legal rulings and theological proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هَذَا، وَالْمُؤَلَّفُ مِنَ الرَّاجِحِ وَالْمَرْجُوحِ مَرْجُوحٌ، فَتَدَبَّرْ. خَاتِمَةٌ: أَجْزَاءُ الْعُلُومِ هِيَ الْمَسَائِلُ، وَالْمَبَادِئُ مِنَ الْوَسَائِلِ.
This, and that which is composed of the preponderant and the preponderated is preponderated, so ponder. Conclusion: The parts of sciences are the problems, and the principles are from the means.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage provides two concluding remarks. First, a logical principle: if a composition consists of both a preponderant (rajih) and a preponderated (marjuh) element, the whole composition is considered preponderated — i.e., the weaker element dominates. This warns against mixing certain and uncertain premises. Second, a structural note: the parts of sciences are the 'problems' (masa'il), while the 'principles' (mabadi') are among the means (wasail) — meaning that principles are tools for solving problems, not ends in themselves.

Key Terms
راجح ومرجوح
مسائل ومبادي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Preponderant
B) Equal
C) Preponderated
D) Unknown

Key Takeaway
A composition of a preponderant and a preponderated element takes the status of the weaker element, a key principle for maintaining epistemic rigor.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
