Second Argument: External Composition Implies Contingency
Second argument against composition with its limitation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the second argument against composition using conditional reasoning and explain why it only refutes external composition.

Core Idea
If the Necessary had parts, it would be dependent and contingent — but that only targets external parts.

The second argument against composition is the simplest: parts create dependence, and dependence implies contingency. However, the commentator notes a crucial limitation — this proof only works against external composition, not mental composition. Let's analyze the logical structure and its scope.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional premise
Find the 'if...then' structure in the Arabic: 'لو كان له أجزاء لكان الواجب محتاجا إليها' (If it had parts, then the necessary would be in need of them).
The antecedent is 'having parts'; the consequent is 'being in need'.
2
Extract the implicit conclusion
The text continues: 'فصار ممكنا' (so it would become possible). This connects need to contingency. The unstated conclusion: the Necessary Being cannot have parts, because it is not possible.
This is a modus tollens: if P then Q; not Q, therefore not P.
3
Understand the commentator's qualification
The commentator says: 'وهذا الدليل إنما ينفي التركيب الخارجي فقط' (This proof only negates external composition). Why? Because mental composition (e.g., genus and differentia) does not create real dependence.
The argument assumes parts are external, separable entities. Mental 'parts' are conceptual.
4
Assess the entailment condition
The commentator adds: 'اللهم إلا أن يبنى على التلازم بينهما' (unless built on entailment between them). This means if external and mental composition are necessarily linked, the argument could extend to mental composition.
This is a dialectical move: the opponent might assert a stronger claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومَنَع الخوانساري قولنا الكثرة المحضة لا ينتزع عنها الواحد مكابرة . نعم يرد عليه النقض بالواجب المباين للمكن مباينة ذاتية ، وسيجيئ تحقيقه إن شاء الله تعالى في موضعه. الثاني: لو كان له أجزاء لكان الواجب محتاجا إليها، فصار ممكنا، وهذا الدليل إنما ينفي التركيب الخارجي فقط ، اللهم إلا أن يبنى على التلازم بينهما . الثالث : لو كان الواجب مركبا لكان فصله المقوم مقسما ؛ لأن الفصل المقوم يحصل الماهية دون الوجود ؛ إذ ماهيته الوجود على ما تقرر في مقره، فيكون محصلا للوجود وهو شأن الفصل المقسم، وهذا مختص بإبطال التركيب الذهني ويرد عليه أن شأن الفصل المقسم أن يكون خارجا عما هو فصل له، ومحصلا لوجوده، ومقسما له إلى قسمين، وفصله المقوم وإن أفاد وجوده إلا أنه ليس خارجا ، حتى يكون مقسما . وتقرير الدفع أنه لو كان مفهوم الواجب عرضا عد الأفراد بسيطة كذلك لكان أمر واحد مشتركا بين هذا الواجب وهذا ، وذلك لأن العرض العام أيضا ماهية من الماهيات، فيكون هناك مبهم ومحصل، فيحتاج، فيكون ممكنا ، هذا خلف ، فإذا لم يكن واجبا لنفسه فكيف لغيره .
The Khwansari prevented our saying: 'The mere multiplicity does not derive from it the one' as stubbornness. Yes, he is refuted by the objection with the necessary being that is essentially distinct from the possible, and its verification will come in its place, God willing. The second: If it had parts, then the necessary would be in need of them, so it would become possible. This proof only negates external composition, unless it is based on the entailment between them. The third: If the necessary were composite, then its constitutive differentia would be divisive; because the constitutive differentia actualizes the quiddity without existence, since its quiddity is existence as established in its place, so it would be an actualizer of existence, which is the function of the divisive differentia. This is specific to refuting mental composition. It is objected that the function of the divisive differentia is to be external to what it is a differentia for, actualizing its existence, and dividing it into two parts; and its constitutive differentia, even if it gives existence, is not external, so as to be divisive. And the explanation of the refutation: If the concept of the necessary were a general accident among simple individuals, then it would be one thing shared between this necessary and that, because the general accident is also a quiddity among quiddities; so there would be ambiguous and actualized, thus in need, thus possible; this is a contradiction. So if it is not necessary for itself, how could it be for another?
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical commentary on the rejection of composition in the Necessary Being. It presents three arguments and their rebuttals, typical of jadal in Islamic theology. The first argument (incomplete) concerns the impossibility of deriving unity from multiplicity; the commentator refutes a criticism by Khwansari using the notion of essential distinction. The second argument is straightforward: if the Necessary Being had parts, it would be dependent on them, thus contingent. The commentator notes that this only negates external composition, and questions the entailment between parts and need. The third argument targets mental composition: if the Necessary Being were composite, its constitutive differentia would be divisive, which is impossible because the constitutive differentia is internal to the quiddity. The rebuttal explains that the constitutive differentia does give existence but is not external, so it cannot be divisive. A further refutation argues that if the concept of the Necessary were a general accident, it would be shared among multiple individuals, leading to ambiguity and need, contradicting necessity. This argument uses the principle that a general accident is a quiddity itself, requiring specification.

Key Terms
التركيب الخارجي (al-tarkib al-khariji): Composition in external reality, where parts exist separately in the world.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the second argument, if the Necessary Being had parts, what would be the immediate consequence?
A) It would be impossible.
B) It would be in need of its parts, making it possible.
C) It would be external composition only.
D) It would be mentally composite.

Key Takeaway
The second argument against composition uses a conditional syllogism (modus tollens) but only refutes external composition, not mental composition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a conditional argument (modus tollens) to prove that the Quran is not created, using the premise: 'If the Quran were created, it would be temporally originated; but the Quran is eternal; therefore it is not created.'
?
Identify which type of composition (external or mental) is refuted by the argument: 'If Allah had location, He would be in need of space; but Allah is self-sufficient; therefore Allah has no location.'

Third Argument: Mental Composition and the Constitutive/Divisive Differentia
Third argument against mental composition and its rebuttal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the third argument against mental composition using the distinction between constitutive and divisive differentiae, and reconstruct the rebuttal involving the general accident.

Core Idea
A composite quiddity with existence as its essence would make the constitutive differentia divisive, which is impossible.

The third argument targets mental composition, specifically the analysis of the Necessary Being into genus and differentia. The commentator argues that if the Necessary were mentally composite, its constitutive differentia would have to be divisive — a contradiction. We'll unpack this subtle ontological argument and the further rebuttal using the concept of a general accident.

Guided Path
1
Understand the distinction between constitutive and divisive differentia
A constitutive differentia (al-fasl al-muqawwim) determines the quiddity and is internal to the species. A divisive differentia (al-fasl al-muqassim) divides a genus into species and is external to the quiddity it divides.
This is a standard distinction in Islamic logic.
2
Apply it to the Necessary Being
The Necessary Being's quiddity is existence itself. If it were composed of genus and differentia, the constitutive differentia would actualize the quiddity. But since the quiddity is existence, the constitutive differentia would be actualizing existence — which is the role of a divisive differentia (which gives actual existence to the species).
The textual claim: 'فيكون محصلا للوجود وهو شأن الفصل المقسم' (so it would be an actualizer of existence, which is the function of the divisive differentia).
3
Understand the rebuttal
The commentator objects: a divisive differentia must be external to what it divides, but the constitutive differentia is internal. So even if it gives existence, it is not external and therefore not divisive.
The rebuttal: 'فصله المقوم وإن أفاد وجوده إلا أنه ليس خارجا، حتى يكون مقسما'
4
Follow the further refutation using general accident
The commentator adds another argument: if the concept of the Necessary were a general accident ('arad 'amm) shared among multiple individuals, it would be a quiddity itself, requiring specification (mubham wa muhassil) and thus would be in need, hence contingent — contradicting necessity.
This is a separate independent argument against mental composition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومَنَع الخوانساري قولنا الكثرة المحضة لا ينتزع عنها الواحد مكابرة . نعم يرد عليه النقض بالواجب المباين للمكن مباينة ذاتية ، وسيجيئ تحقيقه إن شاء الله تعالى في موضعه. الثاني: لو كان له أجزاء لكان الواجب محتاجا إليها، فصار ممكنا، وهذا الدليل إنما ينفي التركيب الخارجي فقط ، اللهم إلا أن يبنى على التلازم بينهما . الثالث : لو كان الواجب مركبا لكان فصله المقوم مقسما ؛ لأن الفصل المقوم يحصل الماهية دون الوجود ؛ إذ ماهيته الوجود على ما تقرر في مقره، فيكون محصلا للوجود وهو شأن الفصل المقسم، وهذا مختص بإبطال التركيب الذهني ويرد عليه أن شأن الفصل المقسم أن يكون خارجا عما هو فصل له، ومحصلا لوجوده، ومقسما له إلى قسمين، وفصله المقوم وإن أفاد وجوده إلا أنه ليس خارجا ، حتى يكون مقسما . وتقرير الدفع أنه لو كان مفهوم الواجب عرضا عد الأفراد بسيطة كذلك لكان أمر واحد مشتركا بين هذا الواجب وهذا ، وذلك لأن العرض العام أيضا ماهية من الماهيات، فيكون هناك مبهم ومحصل، فيحتاج، فيكون ممكنا ، هذا خلف ، فإذا لم يكن واجبا لنفسه فكيف لغيره .
The Khwansari prevented our saying: 'The mere multiplicity does not derive from it the one' as stubbornness. Yes, he is refuted by the objection with the necessary being that is essentially distinct from the possible, and its verification will come in its place, God willing. The second: If it had parts, then the necessary would be in need of them, so it would become possible. This proof only negates external composition, unless it is based on the entailment between them. The third: If the necessary were composite, then its constitutive differentia would be divisive; because the constitutive differentia actualizes the quiddity without existence, since its quiddity is existence as established in its place, so it would be an actualizer of existence, which is the function of the divisive differentia. This is specific to refuting mental composition. It is objected that the function of the divisive differentia is to be external to what it is a differentia for, actualizing its existence, and dividing it into two parts; and its constitutive differentia, even if it gives existence, is not external, so as to be divisive. And the explanation of the refutation: If the concept of the necessary were a general accident among simple individuals, then it would be one thing shared between this necessary and that, because the general accident is also a quiddity among quiddities; so there would be ambiguous and actualized, thus in need, thus possible; this is a contradiction. So if it is not necessary for itself, how could it be for another?
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical commentary on the rejection of composition in the Necessary Being. It presents three arguments and their rebuttals, typical of jadal in Islamic theology. The first argument (incomplete) concerns the impossibility of deriving unity from multiplicity; the commentator refutes a criticism by Khwansari using the notion of essential distinction. The second argument is straightforward: if the Necessary Being had parts, it would be dependent on them, thus contingent. The commentator notes that this only negates external composition, and questions the entailment between parts and need. The third argument targets mental composition: if the Necessary Being were composite, its constitutive differentia would be divisive, which is impossible because the constitutive differentia is internal to the quiddity. The rebuttal explains that the constitutive differentia does give existence but is not external, so it cannot be divisive. A further refutation argues that if the concept of the Necessary were a general accident, it would be shared among multiple individuals, leading to ambiguity and need, contradicting necessity. This argument uses the principle that a general accident is a quiddity itself, requiring specification.

Key Terms
الفصل المقوم (al-fasl al-muqawwim): Constitutive differentia, that which determines the quiddity and distinguishes a species within its genus.
الفصل المقسم (al-fasl al-muqassim): Divisive differentia, that which divides a genus into species and is external to the quiddity.
التركيب الذهني (al-tarkib al-dhihni): Composition in the mind, where a concept is analyzed into genus and differentia.
العرض العام (al-arad al-amm): General accident, a universal predicated of multiple species, itself a quiddity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the third argument, why would the constitutive differentia of a composite Necessary Being be divisive?
A) Because the constitutive differentia gives existence, which is the role of the divisive differentia.
B) Because the constitutive differentia divides the genus into species.
C) Because the Necessary Being has external parts.
D) Because the constitutive differentia is external to the quiddity.

Key Takeaway
The third argument against mental composition fails because it confuses the internal role of the constitutive differentia, but a separate argument using the concept of a general accident successfully refutes mental composition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Apply the distinction to the concept of 'human': identify its constitutive differentia (rationality) and argue whether it is internal or external to the quiddity. Then consider if the same reasoning could be applied to the Necessary Being.
?
Construct a valid modus tollens argument to prove that 'the concept of the Necessary is not a universal accidental concept' using the premise: 'If it were a universal accident, it would need specification and thus be contingent.'

Three Arguments Against Conceiving God's Essence
Commentator explains impossibility of conceiving God's essence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the three logical arguments against the conceivability of God's essence and identify the conditional structure of each.

Core Idea
God's essence cannot be mentally conceived because it would entail logical contradictions.

This passage from a classical logic-text commentary tackles a profound epistemological question: can the human mind grasp the essence of the Necessary Being? The commentator presents three crisp arguments, each showing that mental representation of the divine essence leads to absurdity. By analyzing these arguments, you will see how logical reasoning is applied to safeguard divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central claim
Locate the commentator's assertion about impossibility of conception (taṣawwur) of God's kunnah. Note the phrase 'wa lā yataṣawwaru' and its interpretation as incomprehensibility in the mind.
The claim is that God's essence cannot occur in the mind at all.
2
Analyze the first argument
Assume God's essence occurs in the mind while remaining external. A common factor (mushtarak) would be required, making individuation (taʿayyun) non-unique. This leads to either multiplicity of the particular or its invalidation in the external.
The argument shows a contradiction in the nature of particularity.
3
Analyze the second and third arguments
Second argument: if God's essence occurs in the mind, it becomes a referent (miṣdāq) for both external and mental accidents—impossible. Third argument: it would be both necessary (by essence) and contingent (needing the mind as locus)—a contradiction.
Both arguments rely on the identity of God's essence with its external referent and the predicate 'necessary'.
4
Note the two 'Arshiyan proofs
The commentator mentions two sublime proofs from Ibn ʿArabī's al-Futuḥāt al-Makkiyya, reinforcing the claim from a spiritual perspective, though their content is not elaborated here.
These proofs are considered higher-level insights beyond the logical arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن جملة تقديساته قوله: (ولا يتصور)، إن قرئ بضم علامة بيان استحالة الاستقبال، فمعناه لا يدرك الله تعالى بكنهه؛ لامتناع حصوله في الذهن، ولا شبح له حتى يحصل، ولو فرض لا يكون مبدأ الانكشاف بالضرورة؛ لأن المباين لا يكون مبدأ للانكشاف لمباين آخر. وأما امتناع حصوله في الذهن فمن وجوه، الأول: أنه لو حصل في الذهن فحصوله إما مع بقائه في الخارج، فلا بد من أمر مشترك بينهما واجب عن الآخر، فلا يكون المميز الذي هو التعين عينه. لأحدهما حقيقة، ومميز وأيضا يلزم تكثر الجزئي أو مع بطلانه في الخارج، وهو أشد استحالة ههنا ظهر أيضا أن الجزئي من حيث هو شخص لا يحصل في الذهن، ولا الحاسة حين الإدراك. الثاني: أن مصداق الوجود الخارجي عين ذاته تعالى، من غير حيثية زائدة، فلو حصل في الذهن لكان مصداقا للعوارض الخارجية والذهنية معا. الثالث: أن ذاته مصداق حمل الواجب، بلا حيثية زائدة، فلو حصل في الذهن لكان واجبا ومحتاجا إلى الذهن الذي هو محله. وههنا برهانان عرشيان على امتناع تصوره بكنهه، الأول: ما أفاده الشيخ الأكبر، والإمام الأعظم، سند الأولياء والأتقياء، معدن الهداية خاتم فص الولاية، حسنة من حسنات سيد المرسلين الذي كان وليا وآدم بين الماء والطين، الشيخ محيي الملة والدين محمد بن العربي قدس سره، في (الفتوحات).
And from among His transcendent attributes is His saying: 'And it is not conceived.' If it is read with a damma on the sign of impossibility of reception, then its meaning is: God is not perceived in His essence, due to the impossibility of its occurrence in the mind, and He has no shadow so that it can occur; and even if assumed, it would not be a principle of disclosure necessarily, because the distinct cannot be a principle of disclosure for another distinct. As for the impossibility of its occurrence in the mind, it is from several aspects: First: If it occurs in the mind, then its occurrence is either with its remaining in the external, so there must be a common factor between them that is necessary for the other, so the differentiator which is individuation would not be identical to each one's reality and differentiator. Also, it would entail multiplicity of the particular, or its invalidity in the external, which is more impossible. Here it also appears that the particular insofar as it is a person does not occur in the mind, nor in the sense at the time of perception. Second: That the referent of external existence is identical to His essence with no extra aspect; if it occurred in the mind, it would be a referent for both external and mental accidents. Third: That His essence is the referent for the predicate of the necessary, with no extra aspect; if it occurred in the mind, it would be necessary and in need of the mind which is its locus. And here are two 'Arshiyan proofs on the impossibility of conceiving His essence. The first: That which the Greatest Shaykh, the Imām al-Aʿẓam, the support of the saints and the pious, the mine of guidance, the seal of the bezel of sainthood, a good one from the good deeds of the Master of the Messengers who was a saint while Adam was between water and clay, the reviver of the religion and the community Muḥammad ibn al-ʿArabī, may God sanctify his secret, mentioned in al-Futuḥāt.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This epistemological passage discusses the impossibility of conceiving (taṣawwur) the essence (kunnah) of the Necessary Being. It begins by interpreting the phrase 'wa lā yataṣawwaru' as meaning incomprehensibility in the mind, because God has no shadow or likeness to be represented. The commentator then provides three arguments for why His essence cannot occur in the mind. The first argument: if it occurs in the mind while remaining external, there must be a common factor between the mental and external instances, which would compromise the uniqueness of the particular. Additionally, it would either multiply the particular or invalidate its external existence. The second argument: since God's essence is identical to its external referent without any additional aspect, if it occurred in the mind, it would be a referent for both external and mental accidents simultaneously, which is impossible. The third argument: God's essence is the referent of the predicate 'necessary' without any extra aspect; if it occurred in the mind, it would be both necessary (by nature) and contingent (by needing the mind as a locus), a contradiction. Finally, the commentator mentions two 'Arshiyan (sublime) proofs from Ibn al-ʿArabī's al-Futuḥāt al-Makkiyya, though their content is not elaborated here.

Key Terms
التصور
الكنه
المباين
المشترك
التعين
مصداق

Checkpoint

A) If conceived, God would be divisible.
B) If conceived, God would be both necessary and in need of the mind as a locus.
C) If conceived, God would be subject to external accidents.
D) If conceived, God would not be unique.

Key Takeaway
The three arguments collectively demonstrate that conceiving God's essence is logically impossible, preserving His transcendence (tanzīh).

?
1. Reconstruct the second argument as a conditional syllogism: identify the antecedent ('God's essence occurs in the mind'), the consequent ('it is a referent for both external and mental accidents'), and explain why the consequent is absurd. Conclude that the antecedent must be false.
?

Two Analogies for the Incomprehensibility of God's Essence
Author presents two arguments that God's essence cannot be fully comprehended by reason.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze two analogies for the incomprehensibility of God's essence and explain the core epistemological principle they convey.

Core Idea
God is known not despite His hiddenness but because of His overwhelming clarity.

In this passage, Bahr al-'Ulum expands on the principle that God's essence cannot be fully known by reason. He presents two analogies: one that emphasizes God's absolute dissimilarity from creation, and another that compares divine manifestness to blinding light. Understanding these analogies is crucial for distinguishing between the limits of human reason and the transcendence of God.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base text and its claim
The commentator begins by summarizing a statement from the 'Makkan' source. Find the original claim that God is unlike creation and cannot be represented. Note that this is the foundation of the commentary.
2
Extract the first analogy
The Makkan argues that if creatures can be represented, God cannot, because He does not share any common category. Break down the analogy: what is being compared? Why does representation fail?
3
Extract the second analogy
The First Teacher (Aristotle) uses the analogy of intense light blinding vision. Identify the elements: the extreme manifestness of God and the perplexity of the intellect. How does this analogy differ from the first?
4
Analyze the commentator's expansion
Bahr al-'Ulum adds that the first is 'solid speech' understandable only by a sound heart. What does this imply about the relationship between reason and faith? How does the second analogy reinforce the first?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شرح بحر العلوم على سلم العلوم المكية»: أنه تعالى يخالف المخلوقات، لا مناسبة بينه وبين خلقه البتة ، وكيف لا يشبه من يقبل المثال بمن يقبل المثال ، فالعلم بالله تعالى عزيز عن إدراك العقل والنفس، إلا أنه واجب تعالى وتقدس، وكلما يتلفظ به في حق المخلوقات، أو يتوهم في المركبات وغيرها فالله تعالى في نظر العقل السليم بخلاف ذلك ، لا يجوز عليه الوهم انتهى ملخصا هذا كلام متين ، يعجز عن فهمه إلا من أتى الله بقلب سليم. الثاني ما نقل عن المعلم الأول : أنه سبحانه جلي غاية الجلاء، بحيث لا جلاء فوقه، فيتحير العقل، ويمتنع عن تمام إدراكه كالنور إذا اشتد يمتنع البصر عن الرؤية .
Commentary of Bahr al-'Ulum on Sullam al-'Ulum: 'Makkan': He, exalted, is unlike creation, there is no relationship between Him and His creation at all; for how can one who accepts representation be compared to one who accepts representation? So knowledge of Allah, exalted, is difficult (to obtain) through the perception of intellect and soul, but He is necessary (wajib), exalted and sanctified. And whatever is uttered regarding created things, or imagined regarding composites and others, then Allah, exalted, in the view of the sound intellect is contrary to that; imagination does not apply to Him.' The summary ends. This is solid speech, which only one who comes to Allah with a sound heart can understand. Second: what is transmitted from the First Teacher: that He, glorified, is extremely manifest, to the degree that there is no manifestness above it, so the intellect is perplexed and refrains from fully comprehending Him, like when light intensifies, the eyesight refrains from seeing.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage presents two analogies to explain why the human intellect cannot fully comprehend the essence of Allah. The first analogy, attributed to the 'Makkan' and summarized here, emphasizes that God is utterly unlike any creature. If a creature can be represented in the mind, God cannot, because He does not share any common category. Thus, any attempt to describe God in terms of created things is invalid. The second analogy comes from the 'First Teacher' (Aristotle in the Islamic tradition): God is so manifest (jali) that the intellect is overwhelmed, just as a light so intense that it blinds vision. This shows that the inability to comprehend God is not due to His hiddenness but due to His overwhelming clarity. The passage underscores a key epistemological point: divine transcendence and the limits of human reason.

Key Terms

D) Because God's essence is complex and requires special training.

Key Takeaway
The incomprehensibility of God's essence is due to His utter transcendence and overwhelming manifestness, not any deficiency in His existence.

Refuting the Denial of God's Knowledge: A Logical Analysis
Refutation of Greek denial and introduction of presential knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the Greek argument against God's knowledge and identify the logical flaw in its premise.

Core Idea
The Greeks' denial fails because a relation to creation is not a deficiency for the Creator.

In this passage, the author confronts a bold claim from ancient Greek philosophers who denied God's knowledge outright. They argued that knowledge implies a relation, which they considered a deficiency incompatible with divine perfection. The author dismisses this as absurd and then introduces Suhrawardi's alternative view of presential knowledge (ilm huduri). Let's unpack the logical structure of the Greek argument and see why it collapses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Greek Premises
The Greeks argued: (1) Knowledge requires a relation between knower and known. (2) Any relation implies a deficiency or dependency. From these, they concluded that God cannot have knowledge because He cannot be deficient. Find these two premises in the Arabic passage.
The passage states: 'لأنه إضافة بينه تعالى وبين معلوماته' (because it is a relation between Him and His objects of knowledge).
2
Reconstruct the Formal Syllogism
Write the argument as a categorical syllogism: Major premise: 'Anything that involves a relation is deficient.' Minor premise: 'God's knowledge would involve a relation.' Conclusion: 'Therefore, God's knowledge would be deficient, and since God cannot be deficient, He does not have knowledge.' Check if the conclusion follows logically from the premises.
The syllogism is valid in form, but the truth of the premises is questionable.
3
Identify the Flaw
The flaw lies in the minor premise: Is the relation between God and His creation really a deficiency? In Islamic theology, God's knowledge is creative and causal – He knows things because He brings them into existence, not vice versa. Thus the 'relation' is not one of dependency but of emanation or creativity. The Greeks wrongly assumed all relations imply deficiency.
The author calls this 'great ignorance' (جهل عظيم) and 'absurdity' (سخافة).
4
Understand Suhrawardi's Alternative
Suhrawardi (the martyr) proposed that God's knowledge is presential (huduri): the known object is directly present to the knower without a mediating representation. In this model, there is no 'relation' in the sense of dependency; rather, the divine light illuminates all things. Thus the relational objection does not apply.
The text says: 'ذهب الشيخ المقتول إلى أن علمه تعالى حضوري' (the martyr held that His knowledge is presential).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التفصيل في المقام أن قوما من قدماء جهلة «يونان» نفوا علمه تعالى رأسا ، مستدلين بأن ذلك صفة النقص ؛ لكونه إضافة بينه تعالى وبين معلوماته وهذا جهل عظيم وظلم كبير، وسخافته غنية عن البيان. ثم من سواهم اتفقوا على كونه عالما للأشياء علما تاما، لا يعزب عنه مثقال ذرة. لكن اختلفوا في كيفيته اختلافا عظيما، فذهب الشيخ المقتول إلى أن علمه تعالى حضوري = وبيانه: أنه تعالى نور محض ونور الأنوار، والعالم مفاض عن شعاعه ونهاية العقول ...
The detail in this place: a group of ancient ignorant Greeks denied God's knowledge outright, arguing that knowledge is a deficiency because it is a relation between Him and His objects of knowledge. This is great ignorance and enormous injustice, and its absurdity is needless to expound. Then those besides them agreed that He is knowledgeable of things with perfect knowledge, not even a speck's weight escapes Him. But they differed greatly concerning its modality. The martyr (Suhrawardi) held that His knowledge is presential (huduri), and its explanation: He is pure light and the light of lights, and the world emanates from His ray and the end of intellects...
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage discusses the theological consensus that God knows all things, and refutes the ancient Greek atheists who denied it. The author dismisses their argument as self-evidently absurd. After establishing agreement on divine omniscience, the passage notes that scholars differed on the modality (kayfiyya) of that knowledge. It mentions Suhrawardi's view that God's knowledge is presential (huduri), based on the illuminationist doctrine of God as pure light. The passage is cut off, but it sets up the debate on whether God's knowledge is hululi (conceptual) or huduri (direct presence).

Key Terms
علم حضوري
الشيخ المقتول
إضافة

A) God cannot be known by humans.
B) Knowledge is a relation and relation is a deficiency, so God cannot have knowledge.
C) God's knowledge is indistinguishable from ignorance.
D) The Greeks did not believe in God at all.

Key Takeaway
The Greek denial of God's knowledge is logically flawed because it assumes that any relation to creation is a deficiency, whereas Islamic theology and Illuminationist philosophy offer alternative models (like presential knowledge) that preserve divine perfection.

The Maturidi View on Divine Knowledge and the Philosophical Objection
Maturidi view with objection and response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the Maturidi position on divine knowledge as a relational attribute and explain the philosophical objection regarding relation to the non-existent, along with the Maturidi response.

Core Idea
Knowledge as a relational attribute allows God to know non-existent things without changing His essence.

In this lesson, we examine the Maturidi school's position that divine knowledge is a simple attribute subsisting in God's essence, distinguished only by its relations. We then explore the philosophers' objection that this requires relation to the non-existent and see how the Maturidis defended their view by appealing to the concept of potentiality. Understanding this debate sharpens your ability to analyze theological arguments about God's attributes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Maturidi claim
Find in the Arabic where the author states that the Maturidis hold knowledge to be a simple attribute subsisting in God's essence, with relations that distinguish objects.
The key phrase is 'علمه صفة بسيطة قائمة بذاته'.
2
Extract the philosophers' objection
The philosophers object that this view requires the relation to attach to non-existent things ('تعلق الإضافة بالمعدوم'), since God knows things before they exist.
Look for 'يلزمهم الحكماء بتعلق الإضافة بالمعدوم'.
3
Find the Maturidi response
The Maturidis respond by citing the philosophical concept of potentiality (isti'dad) as a quality in matter that also relates to the non-existent. They also argue that completion by another is not a deficiency.
The response is introduced by 'مستندا بما يقول الفلاسفة'.
4
Evaluate the force of the response
Consider whether the Maturidi defense successfully rebuts the objection. The author notes that no complete proof has been given for unity of knowledge and known, implying the debate remains open.
See 'لم يدل دليل تام على اتحاد العلم أو المعلوم'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مقدمة الكتاب
المعلومات بذاته تعالى ويلزم عليهم مع بطلانه ببراهين قاطعة اتحاد الواجب والممكن ، والمجرد والمادي ، ولعل لكلامهم وجها لستُ أحصله .
وذهب علمائنا الماتريدية عليهم الرحمة إلى أن علمه صفة بسيطة قائمة بذاته تعالى ، ذات إضافة ، ويتميز الأشياء باختلاف إضافاته، ويلزمهم الحكماء بتعلق الإضافة بالمعدوم؛ لأن المعلوم قد يكون معدوما، وهم يلتزمون ذلك، مستندا بما يقول الفلاسفة من أن الاستعداد كيفية في المادة، ذات إضافة متعلقة بالمعدوم ما دام معدوما واستكماله ،بالغير، ويلتزمونه ويقولون الاستكمال بالغير الذي هو صفة كمالية غير نقص، وانكشاف المباين بالمباين، وهم يلتزمون ويقولون: لم يدل دليل تام على اتحاد العلم أو المعلوم، بل فيه مفاسد لا تخفى، ولا يمكن دفعها، كما سيظهر إن شاء الله تعالى .
وذهب المتأخرون إلى أن ذاته تعالى هو العلم، فكما أن الصورة العلمية مبدأ لانكشاف ما هي صورة له، كذلك ذاته مبدأ لانكشاف العالم كله، فهو سبحانه كالصورة العلمية لكل العالم، ويلزمهم انكشاف المباين بالمباين، وكون شيء واحد مبدأ لانكشاف متضادين ومتناقضين ، وأنه تعالى ذو نسبة إلى الكل على السواء، فكيف يكون مبدأ الامتياز ؟
وذهب قوم إلى أن علمه تعالى صور معدومة ذهنا وخارجا كالسراب،
ولا يخفى سخافته .
وذهب الشيخان أبو نصر الفارابي وأبو على إلى أن علمه تعالى بحصول صور المعلومات، وهو علمه الفعلي، ويلزمهما مع عدم مساعدة الأصول الحكمية كما سنشير إليه إن شاء الله تعالى التسلسل؛ لأن تلك الصور غير متناهية حسب عدم تناهي المعلومات، متقدمة على وجود المعلومات أجمع، ولا يمكن أن تصدر عن الواجب دفعة، ولا يلزم صدور الكثرة عن الواحد الحقيقي، فلا بد ههنا من ترتيب على ما نص عليه الشيخ ، فتكون مرتبة .
فهذه تسعة مذاهب، وههنا مذهب آخر لأهل الحق، يقتضي تحقيقه بسطا في الكلام، سنشير إليه إن شاء الله تعالى. هذا ما نبهناك إجمالا ، وإن شئت الزيادة عليه فعليك بمطالعة الكتب المبسوطة .
Introduction to the book. [There is a view that] knowledge is by His essence, and there follows from it, despite its falsity with decisive proofs, the union of the Necessary and the possible, and the abstract and the material. Perhaps their speech has an aspect that I do not comprehend. Our Maturidi scholars, may mercy be upon them, held that His knowledge is a simple attribute subsisting in His essence, having a relation, and things are distinguished by the differences of its relations. The philosophers object to them about the relation attaching to the non-existent because the known may be non-existent, and they accept that, relying on what the philosophers say that potentiality is a quality in matter having a relation attached to the non-existent as long as it is non-existent and its completion by another. They accept that and say: completion by another, which is a perfection attribute, is not deficiency, and the disclosure of the distinct by the distinct. They accept and say: no complete proof has indicated the unity of knowledge and the known; rather there are in it evils not hidden, and they cannot be repelled, as will appear if God wills. The later [philosophers] held that His essence is knowledge. Just as the intellectual form is a principle for the disclosure of what it is a form of, similarly His essence is a principle for the disclosure of the entire world. So He is like the intellectual form for the whole world. They are committed to the disclosure of the distinct by the distinct, and that a single thing is a principle for the disclosure of opposites and contradictories, and that He has a relation to all equally, so how can He be a principle of distinction? A group held that His knowledge is non-existent forms mentally and externally like a mirage, and its absurdity is not hidden. The two shaykhs Abu Nasr al-Farabi and Abu Ali [Ibn Sina] held that His knowledge is by the acquisition of forms of information, and that is His active knowledge. They are committed, without the support of the philosophical principles as we will indicate if God wills, to infinite regress because those forms are infinite according to the infinity of things known, preceding all existence of things known, and they cannot originate from the Necessary in one instance, nor does the emanation of multiplicity from the truly One necessarily follow. So there must be here an order according to what the shaykh stated, so they are ordered. These are nine schools, and here is another school for the People of Truth, requiring expansion in speech for its verification; we will indicate it if God wills. This is what we have alerted you to summarily, and if you wish more, you should consult the detailed books.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage is a survey of theological and philosophical positions on the nature of divine knowledge. It begins with a view (likely a refutation) that identifies God's knowledge with His essence, which leads to unacceptable consequences like necessitating the union of Necessary and possible. Then it presents the Maturidi position: knowledge is an attribute of God, simple and relational, distinguishing objects by relations. The philosophers object that this requires relation to non-existent, but Maturidis respond by citing the philosophers' own theory of potentiality. Next, the later philosophers (Muta'akhkhirun, like Suhrawardi and Mulla Sadra) hold that God's essence is knowledge itself, like an intellectual form disclosing all. The author critiques this by noting it leads to disclosure of distinct entities and opposites. A fourth group absurdly says knowledge is non-existent forms. Finally, al-Farabi and Ibn Sina hold that God's knowledge is by acquired forms, which are His active knowledge, but this leads to infinite regress. The author lists nine schools, but does not detail all, and promises to mention the tenth view of Ahl al-Haqq later.

Key Terms
علمه تعالى
الماتريدية
الإضافة
المعدوم
الاستعداد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main philosophical objection to the Maturidi view mentioned in this passage?
A) It makes God's knowledge corporeal.
B) It requires knowledge to be identical with the known.
C) It requires a relation to attach to non-existent things.
D) It implies that God's knowledge is acquired from outside.

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi view defends divine knowledge as a simple relational attribute, answering the objection from non-existent objects by analogy with potentiality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task: In a theological discussion, a philosopher argues: 'If God knows a non-existent event, then His knowledge must relate to something that does not exist, which is impossible.' Write a short response from a Maturidi perspective, using the reasoning from this passage.
?
Task: Identify the key analogy the Maturidis use (potentiality) and explain why they think it answers the objection.

The Later Philosophers' View: Essence as Knowledge
Later philosophers' view and its critique

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the later philosophers' claim that God's essence is knowledge and identify the critique that this leads to disclosure of opposites and contradictions.

Core Idea
Identifying God's essence with knowledge violates the principle of non-contradiction.

The later philosophers (al-Muta'akhkhirun), such as Suhrawardi and Mulla Sadra, held that God's essence is identical with knowledge itself. They argued that just as an intellectual form discloses what it represents, God's essence discloses the entire world. In this lesson, we examine this view and the author's critique that it entails the absurdity that a single essence discloses opposites and equals simultaneously.

Guided Path
1
State the later philosophers' claim
Find the Arabic where it says 'ذاته تعالى هو العلم' (His essence is knowledge). This is the core claim.
The passage also draws an analogy with the intellectual form.
2
Understand the analogy of the intellectual form
Just as an intellectual form is the principle of disclosure of what it is a form of, so God's essence is the principle of disclosure of the whole world. God is like an intellectual form for all things.
The analogy is in the sentence: 'فكما أن الصورة العلمية مبدأ لانكشاف ما هي صورة له، كذلك ذاته مبدأ لانكشاف العالم كله'.
3
Identify the author's critique
The author points out several problems: it leads to disclosure of the distinct by the distinct, to a single thing being the principle of disclosure of opposites and contradictories, and to God having a relation to all equally, so He cannot be a principle of distinction.
Look for 'ويلزمهم انكشاف المباين بالمباين، وكون شيء واحد مبدأ لانكشاف متضادين ومتناقضين'.
4
Reflect on why this is problematic
If God's essence equally discloses everything, then it fails to account for the differentiation among things. Moreover, disclosing opposites would mean the essence contains contradictory aspects, which is impossible for a simple being.
Consider the theological principle that God is simple and cannot be the source of multiplicity in His essence.

مقدمة الكتاب
المعلومات بذاته تعالى ويلزم عليهم مع بطلانه ببراهين قاطعة اتحاد الواجب والممكن ، والمجرد والمادي ، ولعل لكلامهم وجها لستُ أحصله .
وذهب علمائنا الماتريدية عليهم الرحمة إلى أن علمه صفة بسيطة قائمة بذاته تعالى ، ذات إضافة ، ويتميز الأشياء باختلاف إضافاته، ويلزمهم الحكماء بتعلق الإضافة بالمعدوم؛ لأن المعلوم قد يكون معدوما، وهم يلتزمون ذلك، مستندا بما يقول الفلاسفة من أن الاستعداد كيفية في المادة، ذات إضافة متعلقة بالمعدوم ما دام معدوما واستكماله ،بالغير، ويلتزمونه ويقولون الاستكمال بالغير الذي هو صفة كمالية غير نقص، وانكشاف المباين بالمباين، وهم يلتزمون ويقولون: لم يدل دليل تام على اتحاد العلم أو المعلوم، بل فيه مفاسد لا تخفى، ولا يمكن دفعها، كما سيظهر إن شاء الله تعالى .
وذهب المتأخرون إلى أن ذاته تعالى هو العلم، فكما أن الصورة العلمية مبدأ لانكشاف ما هي صورة له، كذلك ذاته مبدأ لانكشاف العالم كله، فهو سبحانه كالصورة العلمية لكل العالم، ويلزمهم انكشاف المباين بالمباين، وكون شيء واحد مبدأ لانكشاف متضادين ومتناقضين ، وأنه تعالى ذو نسبة إلى الكل على السواء، فكيف يكون مبدأ الامتياز ؟
وذهب قوم إلى أن علمه تعالى صور معدومة ذهنا وخارجا كالسراب،
ولا يخفى سخافته .
وذهب الشيخان أبو نصر الفارابي وأبو على إلى أن علمه تعالى بحصول صور المعلومات، وهو علمه الفعلي، ويلزمهما مع عدم مساعدة الأصول الحكمية كما سنشير إليه إن شاء الله تعالى التسلسل؛ لأن تلك الصور غير متناهية حسب عدم تناهي المعلومات، متقدمة على وجود المعلومات أجمع، ولا يمكن أن تصدر عن الواجب دفعة، ولا يلزم صدور الكثرة عن الواحد الحقيقي، فلا بد ههنا من ترتيب على ما نص عليه الشيخ ، فتكون مرتبة .
فهذه تسعة مذاهب، وههنا مذهب آخر لأهل الحق، يقتضي تحقيقه بسطا في الكلام، سنشير إليه إن شاء الله تعالى. هذا ما نبهناك إجمالا ، وإن شئت الزيادة عليه فعليك بمطالعة الكتب المبسوطة .

6 min
The Shaykhan's View: Active Knowledge and Infinite Regress
Shaykhan's view on active knowledge and its critique

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the position of al-Farabi and Ibn Sina on divine knowledge as active knowledge through acquired forms and explain why the author argues that this leads to infinite regress.

Core Idea
Active knowledge through acquired forms risks infinite regress because the forms are infinite and must be ordered.

Al-Farabi and Ibn Sina (the two shaykhs) held that God's knowledge consists of acquiring the forms of all known things, an active knowledge that precedes their existence. This view, however, faces a serious challenge: since there are infinitely many known things, the forms are infinite, and they cannot all originate from the One at once, leading to an infinite regress. In this lesson, we unpack this argument and its implications for the principle that from the One only one can proceed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Shaykhan's claim
Find in the Arabic where it says that God's knowledge is by the acquisition of forms of information ('حصول صور المعلومات') and that this is His active knowledge ('علمه الفعلي').
The phrase 'وذهب الشيخان أبو نصر الفارابي وأبو على إلى أن علمه تعالى بحصول صور المعلومات'.
2
Understand the concept of active knowledge
Active knowledge means that God's knowledge is the cause of things coming into existence, not merely a reflection. The forms are the patterns by which things are created.
Contrast with passive knowledge that receives from things.
3
Extract the objection of infinite regress
The author argues that because the forms are infinite (since known things are infinite), they cannot all be originated from the Necessary Being in a single act (since only one can proceed from the One). Hence they must be ordered sequentially, leading to an infinite regress.
Look for 'ويلزمهما ... التسلسل؛ لأن تلك الصور غير متناهية'.
4
Reflect on why this is a problem
An infinite regress of causes is impossible in a system that posits a single first cause. This challenges the emanationist framework of the Shaykhan.
The author notes that even al-Farabi and Ibn Sina themselves admitted an order ('ترتيب') to avoid the problem, but that order itself implies multiplicity.

Source Text

Understanding 'لا ينتج' and 'لا يتغير'
Author explains the meaning of 'لا ينتج' and 'لا يتغير'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the correct meaning of 'لا ينتج' and 'لا يتغير' and distinguish them from common misinterpretations.

Core Idea
God's not begetting and not changing affirm His absolute necessity beyond generation and alteration.

In this passage, the author clarifies two divine attributes that are often misunderstood. The first, 'لا ينتج', is not about the lack of proof for God—it means He does not give birth. The second, 'لا يتغير', denies any change in God because He is necessary in all respects.

Guided Path
1
Locate the attributes
Find the phrases 'لا ينتج' and 'لا يتغير' in the passage. Note how they are introduced as part of God's sanctifications (taqdis).
2
Analyze 'لا ينتج'
The author explicitly says it means 'لا يلد' (does not give birth) and warns against reading it as 'no proof'. Why does he reject that interpretation? He says later there will be a proof for God's existence.
The linguistic form 'لا ينتج' is ambiguous; the author settles its meaning theologically.
3
Analyze 'لا يتغير'
The author says God does not change from existence to non-existence or from one attribute to another, because the Necessary Being is necessary from all aspects. This is a standard kalam principle.
Change implies potentiality; God has no potentiality.
4
Apply the principle
How does the principle of necessity (wujub) support these two attributes? Think: if God is necessary, He cannot have originated (be born) or change.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن جملة تقديساته قوله : (لاَ يُنْتِجُ)، أي لا يلد، وهو ظاهر من الأصول التي قررنا لك، وإن قُرِئ بفتح العين، وإن لم يساعده العربية، ففيه إشارة إلى أنه لم يولد لا إلى أنه لا برهان عليه ؛ إذ لا سبب له ، كما زعم بعض الشارحين ؛ لأنه سيجيئ إن شاء الله تعالى أن على وجوده برهانا لميا. ومن جملة تقديساته قوله : (وَلاَ يَتَغَيَّرُ) ، لا من وجود إلى عدم، ولا من صفة إلى أخرى ؛ لأن الواجب واجب من جميع جهاته ، على ما تقرر في مقره. ومن جملة تقديساته قوله : تَعَالَى عَنِ الْجِنْسِ)، أي ما يجانسه ويماثله؛ لأنه قد سبق أن لا شريك له في وجوب وجوده، ولا يماثله ولا يجانسه أحد الممكنات والممتنعات بالضرورة، (وَ) تعالى عن (الْجِهَاتِ) الست، بل الجهات الغير المتناهية؛ لأن التوجه إلى الجهات يستدعي الوضع والتحيز اللذين هما من عوارض الجسم المادي، وهو بريئ عنهما. ولما ذكر بعض التقديسات أراد أن يذكر التمجيدات، واختار واحدا حتى أتى على الوحدانية.
And among His sanctifications is His saying: 'He does not beget', meaning He does not give birth, and it is apparent from the principles we have established for you. If it is read with a fatha on the 'ayn [i.e., 'la yunbitu' meaning 'He does not grow' or similar], even if Arabic does not support it, it contains an indication that He was not begotten, not that there is no proof for Him, because there is no cause for Him, as some commentators claimed. Because it will come, God willing, that there is a proof for His existence from the 'limmi' type. And among His sanctifications is His saying: 'And He does not change', neither from existence to non-existence nor from one attribute to another, because the Necessary is necessary from all aspects, as established in its place. And among His sanctifications is His saying: 'Exalted above genus', i.e., above what is similar and comparable to Him, because it has already been established that He has no partner in the necessity of His existence, and none of the possible and impossible things resemble or compare to Him necessarily. And (He is) exalted above the six directions, rather the infinite directions, because facing directions requires position and spatial occupation, which are accidents of the material body, and He is free from both. And when He mentioned some sanctifications, He intended to mention the glorifications, and He chose one until He came to the unity.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage explains four divine attributes that affirm God's transcendence. The first is 'لا ينتج' (He does not beget), clarified as not giving birth, and the author defends the reading, noting it implies He was not begotten, not that there is no logical proof for Him. The second is 'لا يتغير' (He does not change), meaning no change of existence or attribute because God is necessary in all aspects. The third is 'تعالى عن الجنس' (exalted above genus), denying any similarity or equal. The fourth is 'تعالى عن الجهات' (exalted above directions), because directions entail position and location, which are accidents of material bodies, and God is free from these. The author then notes that after mentioning these sanctifications (tanzih), the poem moves to glorifications (tamjid).

Key Terms
لا ينتج
لا يتغير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the correct meaning of 'لا ينتج'?
A) There is no proof for God
B) He does not beget
C) He does not change
D) He is above genus

Key Takeaway
God's attributes 'لا ينتج' and 'لا يتغير' affirm His absolute perfection and necessity, and must be understood correctly to avoid theological errors.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Explain why the author insists 'لا ينتج' means 'He does not beget' and not 'there is no proof for Him'.
?
Using the principle that God is necessary from all aspects, explain why God cannot change.

Understanding 'تعالى عن الجنس' and 'تعالى عن الجهات'
Author explains the meaning of 'تعالى عن الجنس' and 'تعالى عن الجهات'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why God is exalted above genus and directions, and how these negations protect tawhid.

Core Idea
God's transcendence above genus and directions affirms His uniqueness and non-corporeality.

The final two attributes in the passage declare God's exaltation above genus (jins) and directions (jihaat). The author explains that genus implies similarity, which contradicts God's uniqueness, and directions imply location, which implies a body.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phrases
Find 'تعالى عن الجنس' and 'تعالى عن الجهات' in the passage. They are also part of God's sanctifications.
2
Analyze 'تعالى عن الجنس'
The author says genus (jins) means what is similar and comparable. God is above genus because He has no partner in the necessity of existence, and nothing resembles Him.
In logic, jins is a universal category; here it is used theologically to deny any category for God.
3
Analyze 'تعالى عن الجهات'
The author says directions (jihaat) — the six directions, indeed infinite directions — require position and spatial occupation (al-wad' wa al-tahayyuz), which are accidents of material bodies. God is free from these.
This is a standard kalam argument against anthropomorphism.
4
Synthesize
How do these two negations together protect the concept of tawhid? They exclude any similarity or spatial restriction.

The Peripatetic Argument for Emanation via the First Intellect
The Peripatetic view on emanation: a step-by-step argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the Peripatetic argument for the necessity of a single first intellect and trace how its multiple aspects generate further emanations.

Core Idea
The One's absolute simplicity forces a single first emanation; that emanation's multiple aspects unlock the chain of intellects and spheres.

This lesson unpacks the reasoning of the Peripatetic philosophers (Mashsha'een) as presented in the passage. They start from the principle that the One is absolutely simple and argue that multiplicity cannot directly emanate. By analyzing each step, you will see how they deduce a single First Intellect, which then, through its multiple aspects, becomes the source of all subsequent intellects and spheres.

Guided Path
1
Identify the starting principle
The One is simple from all aspects (بسيط من جميع الجهات). This is the foundational premise.
Simplicity here means no composition or multiplicity in essence.
2
Extract the reasoning against direct emanation
Privations (السلوب) do not multiply the essence, and relations (الإضافات) only occur after the relatum exists. Thus, multiplicity cannot come directly from the One.
The argument rules out any indirect multiplication through mental considerations.
3
Determine the consequence: a single first emanation
Since the One cannot produce multiplicity directly, the first emanation must be a single intellect (العقل الأول), not a body or accident, because bodies are composite and accidents require subjects.
This is a key deductive step.
4
Recognize the multiple aspects of the First Intellect
The First Intellect has quiddity (ماهية), existence from God, necessity from God, and self-intellection. These are distinct aspects that allow further emanations.
These aspects are not in the One but in the first effect.
5
Map how each aspect leads to further emanations
From existence eminates the Second Intellect; from necessity the soul of a sphere; from self-intellection the form; from quiddity the hyle. This process continues down to the Tenth Intellect and the moon sphere, then to matter.
This is a compound causal chain: each intellect's aspects produce the next level.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مقدمة الكتاب يستلزم الكل ، وهو قوله : (جعل) الكليات والجزئيات كلها من غير شيء بإرادة الأبحاث وعناية، على ما يقتضيه الحكمة، وههنا مقامان المقام الأول في كيفية صدور الكثرة عن الواحد الحقيقي، وفيه مذهبان . المتعلقة بالجعل الأول ما ذهب إليه الحكماء. التفصيل : قال المشاؤون لا يمكن أن يصدر عنه الكثرة؛ لبساطته من جميع الجهات، والسلوب عدميات لا تكثر ذات المسلوب عنه، والإضافات لا تحصل إلا بعد حدوث المضاف إليه، فلا بد أن يصدر عنه عقل عقل : لأن الجسم مركب والأعراض والنفوس لا تحدث إلا والموضوع والبدن موجودان ، فلا يصدر كل منهما عن الواحد الحقيقي، ولا بد أن يكون فيه جهات كثيرة ، وإلا لامتنع صدور الكثرة . وإذا تقرر هذا فنقول العقل الأول له ماهية ووجود به تعالى، ووجوب به، وتعقل لنفسه، فمن جهة الوجود فاض عنه تعالى بوساطته العقل الثاني . ومن الجهات الباقية الفلك، فنفسه من جهة الوجوب، وصورته من جهة التعقل ، وهيولاه من جهة ماهيته الأشرف من الأشرف، وكذا من العقل الثاني العقل الثالث وفلك الثوابت، وهكذا إلى أن ينتهى إلى العقل العاشر وفلك القمر، ثم بوساطته عنه تعالى هيولى العناصر والصورة الجسمية والنوعية، على حسب استعداد المادة . ولا يخفى أن علمه تعالى حصولي على رأي شيخهم، فيجوز أن يصدر عنه تعالى كثرة ، من غير وساطة شيء سوى الصور العلمية ؛ لوجدان التكثر في صفاته بحلول الصور الكثيرة. قال الإشراقية : إن كثرة جهات العقول مع كونها اعتبارية لا تفي؛ لأن الكواكب الثوابت سبب كونها متخالفة الماهية والأحياز غير محصورة، فلا بد لها من علل كثيرة كذلك ، وتلك الجهات محصورة ، أن المشائين أيضا لم يجزموا بكون العقول عشرة أو عشرين، وبكون الأفلاك تسعة ، فالطريق على رأيهم أنه صدر عنه تعالى معلول، وليكن (أ)، وصدر عنه تعالى بوساطة (أ) (ب)، وبوساطة (ب) (ج) ، وبوساطة (أ ب) معا (د) و (ج) في مرتبة واحدة وبوساطة (د) معلول، وبوساطة (ج) ثان ، وبوساطة (د) ثالث ، وبوساطة (د ب رابع ، وبوساطة (دج) خامس، وبوساطة (ج أ) سادس، وبوساطة (ج) (ب) سابع ، وبوساطة أ ب ج) ثامن، وبوساطة (أ ب د تاسع ، وبوساطة (أ) ب ج ) عاشر . وهذه العشرة في مرتبة، وهكذا بالغا ما بلغ. وقالوا جميعا لربط الحادث بالقديم إن حركة الفلك أزلية أبدية متجددة، فهي ذات جهتين، فمن جهة دوامها فاضت عن القديم، ومن جهة التجدد صارت واسطة بين الحوادث وبين الله تعالى في وصول الفيض.
Introduction of the book: the whole necessitates all, and it is his statement: 'making' the universals and particulars all from nothing by will of research and care, according to what wisdom requires. Here are two stations: the first station on how multiplicity emanates from the true One, and in it are two schools. The school related to the making: the first is what the philosophers went to. Detail: The Peripatetics said that multiplicity cannot emanate from Him because of His simplicity from all aspects, and privations are non-entitative and do not multiply the essence of the one from whom it is stripped, and relations do not obtain except after the occurrence of the relatum. So necessarily there emanates from Him an intellect: because the body is composite, and accidents and souls do not occur except when the subject and the body exist, so neither of them emanates from the true One. And it must have many aspects, otherwise emanation of multiplicity would be impossible. And when this is established, we say: the First Intellect has a quiddity and existence through Him, and necessity through Him, and intellection of itself. So from the aspect of existence, by His mediation, the Second Intellect emanates from Him. And from the remaining aspects, the sphere: its soul from the aspect of necessity, its form from the aspect of intellection, its hyle from the aspect of its quiddity, the nobler from the nobler. And likewise from the Second Intellect, the Third Intellect and the sphere of fixed stars, and so on until it ends at the Tenth Intellect and the sphere of the moon. Then by His mediation from Him the hyle of the elements and the corporeal and specific form, according to the preparedness of matter. And it is not hidden that His knowledge is acquired in the view of their master, so it is possible that multiplicity emanates from Him without mediation of anything except the intellectual forms, because of finding multiplicity in His attributes through the indwelling of many forms. The Illuminationists said: Indeed the multitude of aspects of the intellects, while being mental considerations, do not suffice; because the fixed stars, the reason for their being diverse in quiddity and positions is uncountable, so they must have many causes likewise, and those aspects are countable. That the Peripatetics also did not affirm that the intellects are ten or twenty, and that the spheres are nine. So the way according to them is that a single effect emanated from Him, let it be (a), and from Him by mediation of (a) (b), and by mediation of (b) (c), and by mediation of (a) and (b) together (d) and (c) in one rank, and by mediation of (d) an effect, and by mediation of (c) a second, and by mediation of (d) a third, and by mediation of (d)(b) a fourth, and by mediation of (d)(c) a fifth, and by mediation of (c)(a) a sixth, and by mediation of (c)(b) a seventh, and by mediation of (a)(b)(c) an eighth, and by mediation of (a)(b)(d) a ninth, and by mediation of (a)(b)(c) a tenth. And these ten are in one rank, and so on ad infinitum. And they all said, for connecting the contingent with the eternal, that the motion of the sphere is eternal, perpetual, renewing, so it has two aspects: from the aspect of its continuity it flowed from the eternal, and from the aspect of renewal it became a mediator between contingent events and God in the flow of emanation.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage is an expansion from a commentary on a logic text, but it delves into metaphysical cosmology. The author introduces the problem of how multiplicity (the many) can emanate from the One, who is absolutely simple. Two schools are mentioned: the Peripatetics (Mashsha'een) and the Illuminationists (Ishraqis). The author first presents the Peripatetic view. They argue that multiplicity cannot directly emanate from the One because the One is simple, and privations and relations do not multiply its essence. Therefore, the first emanation must be a single intellect. This first intellect, being the effect of the One, has multiple aspects: its quiddity, its existence which is from God, its necessity (also from God), and its self-intellection. These aspects become the sources for further emanations. From one aspect (existence), the Second Intellect emanates; from other aspects, the soul, form, and hyle of an astral sphere flow. This process continues down to the Tenth Intellect and the sphere of the moon, after which material elements emanate. The author notes that in the Peripatetic view, God's knowledge is acquired (through forms), which allows multiplicity in His knowledge without mediation. Then the author presents the Illuminationist critique: the aspects of the intellects are mental considerations and cannot account for the vast diversity of fixed stars, which are uncountable. The Illuminationists also point out that the Peripatetics themselves are not certain about the exact number of intellects or spheres. Thus, the Peripatetics propose a hierarchical emanation using a notation of variables (a, b, c, d) to symbolize successive effects. Finally, the author mentions a shared doctrine: the motion of the sphere is both eternal and renewing, serving as a bridge between the eternal and the temporal.

Key Terms
المشاؤون
العقل الأول
الوجود والوجوب
هيولى

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because bodies are composite and accidents require a subject, so only an intellect can emanate directly
C) Because the First Intellect already contains all multiplicity
D) Because the One's knowledge is acquired

Key Takeaway
The Peripatetics argue that the One's simplicity necessitates a single first intellect, whose multiple aspects then enable a chain of emanations.

?
Using the same reasoning, explain why the First Intellect must have multiple aspects for further emanations.

The Illuminationist Critique and Notational Emanation Model
The Illuminationist critique and their notational emanation model.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Illuminationists' objection to the Peripatetic emanation scheme and describe their alternative notational system for generating multiple effects.

Core Idea
The Illuminationists argue that mental aspects cannot account for the vast diversity of fixed stars, and they propose a flexible notational system that can generate any number of effects.

This lesson presents the Ishraqi (Illuminationist) critique of the Peripatetic model. They point out a key weakness: the aspects of the intellects are mental considerations and are limited, but the diversity of the fixed stars is uncountable. They also note that the Peripatetics themselves are uncertain about the exact numbers. As an alternative, they offer a notational method using variables (أ, ب, ج, د) to demonstrate how an unlimited number of effects can emanate in a hierarchical yet flexible manner.

Guided Path
1
Identify the object of critique
The Illuminationists target the Peripatetic claim that the aspects of the intellects provide sufficient grounds for the multiplicity of the celestial spheres.
The Peripatetics used aspects like existence, necessity, quiddity, intellection.
2
Understand the objection
The aspects are mental considerations (اعتبارية) and countable, but the fixed stars are diverse in quiddity and positions and are not limited. Therefore, the aspects are insufficient.
This is an empirical insufficiency objection: the Peripatetic scheme cannot explain the observed diversity.
3
Acknowledge the uncertainty argument
The Peripatetics themselves are not certain whether the intellects are ten or twenty, or the spheres nine. This shows that their scheme is not fixed.
This undermines the claim that the number of intellects is determined by the aspects.
4
Learn the Illuminationist alternative: notational emanation
They propose that from the One a single effect (أ) emanates. Then from (أ) another (ب) emanates, from (ب) (ج), and from combinations of earlier effects (e.g., (أ و ب معا) produce (د) and (ج) in the same rank). This continues to generate many effects.
The notation uses Arabic letters as placeholders for intellects or effects.
5
Generalize the pattern
The system allows unlimited ranks: ten effects in one rank, and so on ad infinitum. This contrasts with the fixed ten-intellect scheme.
This shows that a flexible notational model can account for any multiplicity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

. المتعلقة بالجعل الأول ما ذهب إليه الحكماء. التفصيل : قال المشاؤون لا يمكن أن يصدر عنه الكثرة؛ لبساطته من جميع الجهات، والسلوب عدميات لا تكثر ذات المسلوب عنه، والإضافات لا تحصل إلا بعد حدوث المضاف إليه، فلا بد أن يصدر عنه عقل عقل : لأن الجسم مركب والأعراض والنفوس لا تحدث إلا والموضوع والبدن موجودان ، فلا يصدر كل منهما عن الواحد الحقيقي، ولا بد أن يكون فيه جهات كثيرة ، وإلا لامتنع صدور الكثرة . وإذا تقرر هذا فنقول العقل الأول له ماهية ووجود به تعالى، ووجوب به، وتعقل لنفسه، فمن جهة الوجود فاض عنه تعالى بوساطته العقل الثاني . ومن الجهات الباقية الفلك، فنفسه من جهة الوجوب، وصورته من جهة التعقل ، وهيولاه من جهة ماهيته الأشرف من الأشرف، وكذا من العقل الثاني العقل الثالث وفلك الثوابت، وهكذا إلى أن ينتهى إلى العقل العاشر وفلك القمر، ثم بوساطته عنه تعالى هيولى العناصر والصورة الجسمية والنوعية، على حسب استعداد المادة . ولا يخفى أن علمه تعالى حصولي على رأي شيخهم، فيجوز أن يصدر عنه تعالى كثرة ، من غير وساطة شيء سوى الصور العلمية ؛ لوجدان التكثر في صفاته بحلول الصور الكثيرة. قال الإشراقية : إن كثرة جهات العقول مع كونها اعتبارية لا تفي؛ لأن الكواكب الثوابت سبب كونها متخالفة الماهية والأحياز غير محصورة، فلا بد لها من علل كثيرة كذلك ، وتلك الجهات محصورة ، أن المشائين أيضا لم يجزموا بكون العقول عشرة أو عشرين، وبكون الأفلاك تسعة ، فالطريق على رأيهم أنه صدر عنه تعالى معلول، وليكن (أ)، وصدر عنه تعالى بوساطة (أ) (ب)، وبوساطة (ب) (ج) ، وبوساطة (أ ب) معا (د) و (ج) في مرتبة واحدة وبوساطة (د) معلول، وبوساطة (ج) ثان ، وبوساطة (د) ثالث ، وبوساطة (د ب رابع ، وبوساطة (دج) خامس، وبوساطة (ج أ) سادس، وبوساطة (ج) (ب) سابع ، وبوساطة أ ب ج) ثامن، وبوساطة (أ ب د تاسع ، وبوساطة (أ) ب ج ) عاشر . وهذه العشرة في مرتبة، وهكذا بالغا ما بلغ. وقالوا جميعا لربط الحادث بالقديم إن حركة الفلك أزلية أبدية متجددة، فهي ذات جهتين، فمن جهة دوامها فاضت عن القديم، ومن جهة التجدد صارت واسطة بين الحوادث وبين الله تعالى في وصول الفيض.
Introduction of the book: the whole necessitates all, and it is his statement: 'making' the universals and particulars all from nothing by will of research and care, according to what wisdom requires. Here are two stations: the first station on how multiplicity emanates from the true One, and in it are two schools. The school related to the making: the first is what the philosophers went to. Detail: The Peripatetics said that multiplicity cannot emanate from Him because of His simplicity from all aspects, and privations are non-entitative and do not multiply the essence of the one from whom it is stripped, and relations do not obtain except after the occurrence of the relatum. So necessarily there emanates from Him an intellect: because the body is composite, and accidents and souls do not occur except when the subject and the body exist, so neither of them emanates from the true One. And it must have many aspects, otherwise emanation of multiplicity would be impossible. And when this is established, we say: the First Intellect has a quiddity and existence through Him, and necessity through Him, and intellection of itself. So from the aspect of existence, by His mediation, the Second Intellect emanates from Him. And from the remaining aspects, the sphere: its soul from the aspect of necessity, its form from the aspect of intellection, its hyle from the aspect of its quiddity, the nobler from the nobler. And likewise from the Second Intellect, the Third Intellect and the sphere of fixed stars, and so on until it ends at the Tenth Intellect and the sphere of the moon. Then by His mediation from Him the hyle of the elements and the corporeal and specific form, according to the preparedness of matter. And it is not hidden that His knowledge is acquired in the view of their master, so it is possible that multiplicity emanates from Him without mediation of anything except the intellectual forms, because of finding multiplicity in His attributes through the indwelling of many forms. The Illuminationists said: Indeed the multitude of aspects of the intellects, while being mental considerations, do not suffice; because the fixed stars, the reason for their being diverse in quiddity and positions is uncountable, so they must have many causes likewise, and those aspects are countable. That the Peripatetics also did not affirm that the intellects are ten or twenty, and that the spheres are nine. So the way according to them is that a single effect emanated from Him, let it be (a), and from Him by mediation of (a) (b), and by mediation of (b) (c), and by mediation of (a) and (b) together (d) and (c) in one rank, and by mediation of (d) an effect, and by mediation of (c) a second, and by mediation of (d) a third, and by mediation of (d)(b) a fourth, and by mediation of (d)(c) a fifth, and by mediation of (c)(a) a sixth, and by mediation of (c)(b) a seventh, and by mediation of (a)(b)(c) an eighth, and by mediation of (a)(b)(d) a ninth, and by mediation of (a)(b)(c) a tenth. And these ten are in one rank, and so on ad infinitum. And they all said, for connecting the contingent with the eternal, that the motion of the sphere is eternal, perpetual, renewing, so it has two aspects: from the aspect of its continuity it flowed from the eternal, and from the aspect of renewal it became a mediator between contingent events and God in the flow of emanation.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage is an expansion from a commentary on a logic text, but it delves into metaphysical cosmology. The author introduces the problem of how multiplicity (the many) can emanate from the One, who is absolutely simple. Two schools are mentioned: the Peripatetics (Mashsha'een) and the Illuminationists (Ishraqis). The author first presents the Peripatetic view. They argue that multiplicity cannot directly emanate from the One because the One is simple, and privations and relations do not multiply its essence. Therefore, the first emanation must be a single intellect. This first intellect, being the effect of the One, has multiple aspects: its quiddity, its existence which is from God, its necessity (also from God), and its self-intellection. These aspects become the sources for further emanations. From one aspect (existence), the Second Intellect emanates; from other aspects, the soul, form, and hyle of an astral sphere flow. This process continues down to the Tenth Intellect and the sphere of the moon, after which material elements emanate. The author notes that in the Peripatetic view, God's knowledge is acquired (through forms), which allows multiplicity in His knowledge without mediation. Then the author presents the Illuminationist critique: the aspects of the intellects are mental considerations and cannot account for the vast diversity of fixed stars, which are uncountable. The Illuminationists also point out that the Peripatetics themselves are not certain about the exact number of intellects or spheres. Thus, the Peripatetics propose a hierarchical emanation using a notation of variables (a, b, c, d) to symbolize successive effects. Finally, the author mentions a shared doctrine: the motion of the sphere is both eternal and renewing, serving as a bridge between the eternal and the temporal.

Critique of the Three-Chain Solution
Critique of the Peripatetic three-chain solution

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the author's objection to the three-chain theory and evaluate its logical strength.

Core Idea
The three-chain theory fails because it either forces coexistence of motions or leaves the chain contingent.

The Peripatetic philosophers proposed a three-chain theory to explain how temporal events can arise from an eternal cause without infinite regress. The author of this passage subjects that theory to a rigorous critique. In this lesson, we will unpack the structure of the three chains and follow the author's step-by-step refutation. Understanding this critique sharpens your ability to detect hidden assumptions in causal arguments, a skill essential in Kalam and Usul al-Fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three chains
The passage mentions three chains: (1) partial imaginings (takhayyulat), (2) wills and yearnings (iradat wa-ashwaq), and (3) rotations (dawrat). Note the causal relationship: an imagining gives rise to a will, which gives rise to a rotation, which then leads to another imagining, and so on.
The chain is meant to avoid direct infinite regress in motion.
2
Analyze the author's disjunction
The author presents a dilemma: the rotation that emanates from a partial will is either an obligating cause (علة موجبة) for the next imagining or merely an efficient cause (علة معدّة). Consider each horn separately.
The distinction between obligating and preparatory cause is crucial in Islamic philosophy.
3
Evaluate the first horn (obligating cause)
If the rotation is an obligating cause, it must coexist with its effect (the next imagining) and with all subsequent effects. This would force all parts of motion to exist simultaneously, which contradicts the temporal nature of motion.
Coexistence of all parts of motion is impossible because motion has sequential parts.
4
Evaluate the second horn (efficient cause)
If the rotation is merely an efficient cause, then the entire chain of efficient causes must terminate in the Necessary Existence (واجب الوجود بالذات) to avoid pre-eternity. But then the existence of any particular link is not necessary—the whole chain could have been non-existent, so the required motion would not necessarily exist.
This argument relies on the principle that a thing does not exist until it becomes necessary.
5
Draw the conclusion
Both horns lead to absurdity: the first implies timeless coexistence of temporal parts, the second undermines the necessity of the causal link. Therefore, the three-chain theory fails to explain the connection between the eternal and the temporal.
The author thus rejects the entire scheme and refers to another work for elaboration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
