Seven Objections Against the Identity of Genus and Matter
Author refutes a philosophical claim about genus and matter with seven objections

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and reconstruct the seven objections raised against the claim that genus and matter are identical, and assess their logical force.

Core Idea
The claim that genus and matter are identical collapses under seven independent metaphysical and logical objections.

In this passage, the author (a commentator) refutes a philosophical position that equates genus (jins) with matter (māddah). This is a classic example of critical refinement in the Islamic logical tradition. You will learn how to dissect a refutation by extracting each objection, understanding its grounds, and evaluating its strength. This skill is essential for engaging in scholarly debate (munāẓara) and for testing the robustness of any theoretical claim.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target claim
Find the exact statement being refuted in the Arabic. It appears in the gloss: 'al-jins wa al-māddah mutaḥaddān bi al-dhāt' (genus and matter are identical in essence).
The claim is a metaphysical identification, not a linguistic one.
2
Extract each objection (ammā awwalan... ammā sābi‘an)
List the seven objections in order. Note the key point of each: temporal vs absolute, infinite regress, ambiguity of 'matter', composition of soul, denial of actual species for ten intellects, denial of point's simplicity, and the example being replaceable.
The objections are numbered from first to seventh.
3
Analyze the grounds of each objection
For each objection, determine whether it is based on temporal logic, terminological precision, metaphysical consistency, or appeal to established doctrines.
Some objections rely on rejecting Peripatetic positions; others are purely logical.
4
Evaluate the overall refutation
Consider whether the objections succeed in disproving the original claim. Are any of them weaker? Which rely on external assumptions?
The author concludes with a humble appeal to divine knowledge, indicating a measure of humility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الكليات الخمس
الحاشية: الأول هو الحق من وجه ، يعني نظرا إلى مفهومها في بادئ الرأي، وأما النظر الدقيق فيقتضي الإطلاق ؛ فإن كل حادث ولو ذاتيا فهو مسبوق بمادة بالضرورة الوجدانية ، والجنس والمادة متحدان بالذات، على ما عرفت . يرد النفس الناطقة ؛ لأنا لا نقول بتجردها من كل وجه ، بل أمر بين بين، وله حظ من الجسمية التي هي مادتها وجنسها . ولا يرد العقول العشرة ؛ فإنا لا نسلم كونها أنواعا محصلة، بل مراتب عقلية ومباد كلية وإن كانت موجودة فتوسطها في آثار الفيض كتوسط الأجناس المتوسطة. وأما النقطة فعلى تقدير وجودها أنها بسيطة خارجا، وأما ذهنا فممنوع ، كيف والبساطة مطلقا من صفات الله تعالى ، فتدبر ، اهـ . ولا يخفى ما فيه من الاختلال. أما أولا فلأن مسبوقية كل حادث بالمادة لو سلمت فإنما يسلم في الحادث الزماني، كما هو رأيهم، لا مطلقا، كيف وهو خلاف ما هم عليه ، ودعوى الضرورة غير مسموعة. وأما ثانيا : فلأن ذلك يؤدي إلى التسلسل المحال. وبيانه: أن المادة لا تكون واجبة بالذات، بل ممكنة، فيكون حادثة بالذات، ولا بد لها من مادة ، وهي أيضا كذلك ، وهكذا إلى ما لا يتناهى. وأما ثالثا فلأن المادة المطلقة في تلك المسألة أعم من المادة المقومة ؛ فإنهم قالوا فيها: إن كان الحادث صورة فالمادة الهيولى، وإن كان عرضا فالمادة الموضوع، وإن كان نفسا فالمادة البدن ، فقوله: الجنس والمادة متحدان غير سديد. وأما رابعا: فلان جنسية الجسم للنفس يوجب تركبها في الخارج، وكونها جسما في حد الذات لكون الكل جسما، ويلزم انعدامها بانعدام الجسم ؛ ضرورة انتفاء الكل عند انتفاء الجزء، وكل ذلك خلاف التحقيق، وخلاف ما تقرر في مدارك الحكماء ، فقوله : ولا يرد النفس الناطقة إلخ ليس بشيء. نعم، لو قال ههنا كما قال في النقطة لكان له وجه ويرد عليه ما يجيئ فيه . وأما خامسا فلأن قوله لا نسلم كونها أنواعا إلخ منع لمسألة عظيمة متقررة عندهم . وأما سادسا: فلأن المنع المرموز في قوله: وأما النقطة فعلى تقدير وجودها منع لما هو متقرر في مدارك المشائين . وأما سابعا: فلأن النقطة كانت مثالا للنوع البسيط في كلام القوم، فمنع بساطتها غير مضر ؛ فإن لنا أن نبدل بالوجود هذا والعلم الحق عند علام الغيوب .
Chapter on the five universals. The gloss: The first is true in a sense, meaning considering its concept at first glance. But precise consideration demands absolute [generality]; for every event, even if essential, is preceded by matter by self-evident necessity, and genus and matter are identical in essence, as you know. The rational soul is an objection; for we do not say it is abstract from every aspect, but a between-between thing, and it has a share of corporeality which is its matter and genus. The ten intellects are not an objection; for we do not concede that they are actual species, but rather intellectual ranks and universal principles; even if they exist, their mediation in the effects of emanation is like the mediation of intermediate genera. As for the point, on the assumption of its existence, it is simple externally, but mentally it is denied; how can it be, while absolute simplicity is an attribute of God Almighty? So reflect. End. It is not hidden what disorder is in it. First: because the priority of every event to matter, if conceded, is only conceded in temporal events, as is their view, not absolutely; how can it be while it contradicts what they hold? And the claim of necessity is not acceptable. Second: because that leads to impossible regress. Its explanation: matter is not necessary by essence, but possible, so it is an event by essence, and it must have matter, and that likewise, ad infinitum. Third: because absolute matter in that issue is more general than constitutive matter; for they said about it: if the event is a form, its matter is hyle; if it is an accident, its matter is the subject; if it is a soul, its matter is the body. So his saying 'genus and matter are identical' is not sound. Fourth: because the genus of body for the soul necessitates its composition in externals, and its being a body in the essence of self because the whole is a body, and its non-existence follows from the non-existence of the body, by necessity of the whole ceasing when the part ceases, and all that contradicts verification and what is established in the understandings of the philosophers. So his saying 'the rational soul is not an objection' etc. is nothing. Yes, if he had said here as he said about the point, it would have a face, and there would be an objection against it that will come. Fifth: because his saying 'we do not concede that they are species' etc. is a denial of a great matter established among them. Sixth: because the denial hinted at in his saying 'as for the point, on the assumption of its existence' is a denial of what is established in the understandings of the Peripatetics. Seventh: because the point was an example of the simple species in the speech of the people, so denying its simplicity is not harmful; for we can substitute existence for it. And the true knowledge is with the Knower of the Unseen.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hāshiya) refuting a previous claim that 'genus and matter are identical in essence'. The author presents seven objections (amman awwalan through amman sābiʻan) dismantling this claim from different angles. First objection: The claim's universality fails because the priority of events to matter only holds for temporal events, not absolutely. Second: The claim leads to infinite regress, as matter would itself be an event requiring matter. Third: The term 'matter' is ambiguous; absolute matter is more general than constitutive matter, which varies for forms, accidents, and souls. Fourth: If body is a genus for the soul, the soul would be composite and cease with the body, contradicting philosophical consensus. Fifth: Denying the ten intellects are actual species contradicts established doctrines. Sixth: Denying the point's simplicity is a denial of Peripatetic positions. Seventh: The point was merely an example of a simple species; denying its simplicity is irrelevant. The author concludes with a humble appeal to divine knowledge.

Key Terms
الحادث
المادة
الجنس
النقطة
البدن
الهيولى
الجسمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the third objection, why is the claim 'genus and matter are identical' unsound?
A) Because matter is always hyle, not genus.
B) Because absolute matter is more general than constitutive matter, and they differ in different cases.
C) Because the soul is not a body, so genus cannot apply.
D) Because the ten intellects refute the claim.

Key Takeaway
The seven objections showcase how a seemingly simple identification can be dismantled by careful analysis of temporal scope, term ambiguity, and metaphysical implications.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Simplicity of Existence: A Conditional Reductio Argument
Author argues for the simplicity of existence by reductio ad absurdum and answers objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the conditional proof for the simplicity of existence and identify the principle of talazum used to respond to objections.

Core Idea
Existence cannot be composite; otherwise absurdities follow.

In this passage, the author argues for the simplicity of existence using a conditional reductio: if existence were composite, then an accident would be an attribute of itself or lead to a conjunction of opposites. The argument interacts with objections from the 'Old Commentaries' and employs the principle of talazum (mental-external correlation) to defend the conclusion. Understanding this proof sharpens your ability to use conditional reasoning in theological contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target claim
What is the author trying to prove? The claim is that existence (al-wujud) is simple, not composite.
2
Extract the conditional structure
Find the 'if-then' form: 'If existence were composite, then absurdities follow.' This is a conditional proof.
3
Trace the absurd consequences
What specific absurdities follow? The whole would be an attribute of a part, an accident would be of itself, or there would be a conjunction of opposites and infinite regress.
4
Examine the objection and response
The objection offers two interpretations of the condition. The response uses talazum: mental parts must correspond to external parts, so composition leads to distinct existents and an infinite regress is impossible, forcing a single part that is subject to existence.
5
Evaluate the final qualification
The author notes that if existence is the simple concept (masdari), no proof is needed; but for real simplicity (haqiqi), the proof only holds if the commonality (ishtirak) of existence is established.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيكون الكل صفة للجزء، لكن لا يكون ذلك الجزء صفة لنفسه، بل يكون صفة لسائر الأجزاء، فلا يكون العارض بتمامه عارضا، أو بالعدم، فيلزم اجتماع النقيضين. وأُورِدَ عليه أمور ، منها ما في «الحواشي القديمة»: أنه إن أراد أنه يجب أن يكون أجزاء العارض بأسرها عارضة لمعروض ذلك العارض فينقض بالكثرة ؛ فإنها عارضة للمجموع، مع أن الوحدة التي هي جزء لها ليست عارضة له بتمامه ، بل لجزئه، وإن أراد أنه يجب أن يكون أجزاء العارض عارضة، أما للمعروض أو جزئه فلأحد أن يلتزم كون الوجود عارضا لجزئه، وجزئه لجزئه وجزء جزئه لجزء جزئه ، فتأمل ؛ فإن فيه مجالا للتفكر. ويمكن أن يجاب - بناء على التلازم - بأن الأجزاء العقلية يجب يحاذيه الأجزاء الخارجية، فلو كان الوجود مركبا فأجزاءه تكون موجودة مميزة، وإذ بطل غير المتناهي فلا بد من جزء واحد يعرض الوجود له، فإما بالكلية فيلزم عروض الشيء لنفسه أو بعضه، فلا يكون العارض بتمامه عارضا، وفي هذا الدليل دقائق . والحق أنه إن أريد بساطة الوجود المصدري البديهي التصور فغنية عن البيان ، وإن أريد بساطة الحقيقي فمشكل جدا، إلا إذا ثبت اشتراكه، فحينئذ يتم هذا الدليل، والله أعلم بالصواب.
Thus the whole is an attribute of the part, but that part is not an attribute of itself; rather it is an attribute of the other parts. So the accident in its entirety is not accidental, or else [it is] due to non-existence, leading to the conjunction of two opposites. Objections were raised against it, including what is in the 'Old Commentaries': that if he means that it is necessary that all parts of the accident be accidental to the subject of that accident, then it is refuted by multiplicity; for it is accidental to the whole, while the unity which is a part of it is not accidental to the whole, but to its part. And if he means that it is necessary that parts of the accident be accidental, either to the subject or to its part, then one may accept that existence is accidental to its part, and its part to its part, and the part of its part to the part of its part. So ponder; for in it there is room for thought. It can be answered – based on correlation – that mental parts must correspond to external parts. So if existence were composite, its parts would be distinct existents. Since the infinite is invalid, there must be a single part to which existence is accidental. Thus either [accidental] to the whole, necessitating that a thing be accidental to itself or to a part, so the accident in its entirety would not be accidental. In this proof there are subtleties. The truth is that if by existence they mean the simple thing known by definition that is self-evident in conception, it is in no need of explanation. But if they mean real simplicity, it is very problematic, unless the commonality [of existence] is proven; then this proof is valid. And God knows best what is correct.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for the simplicity of existence (wujud) using a reductio ad absurdum argument. The author begins by showing that if existence were composite, then the whole would be an attribute of a part, leading to absurdities such as the conjunction of opposites. Then an objection from the 'Old Commentaries' is presented: the argument might be interpreted in two ways, both of which have counterexamples. The first interpretation is that all parts of the accident must be accidental to the same subject; this is refuted by the case of multiplicity, where unity is part of multiplicity but not accidental to the whole. The second interpretation is that parts can be accidental to the subject or its part; this is conceded but leads to an infinite regress. The author then provides a response based on the principle of correlation (talazum): mental parts must correspond to external parts. If existence were composite, its parts would be distinct existents, and since an infinite regress is impossible, there must be a single part that is the subject of existence. This leads to the conclusion that existence cannot be composite. The author then qualifies: if existence is understood as the simple concept (al-wujud al-masdari), no proof is needed; but if it is real simplicity, the proof only works if the commonality of existence is established. This is a nuanced epistemological discussion about the nature of existence.

Key Terms
الوجود
العارض
التلازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the logical structure of the author's main argument against the composition of existence?
A) Categorical syllogism with universal premises
B) Conditional proof: if existence were composite, then absurdities follow; therefore existence is simple
C) Immediate inference by conversion
D) Analogical reasoning from other accidents

Key Takeaway
The simplicity of existence is proven by a conditional reductio that shows composition leads to logical impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the theological statement: 'If the world were eternal, then it would have a cause.' Identify the antecedent and consequent, then determine if the argument uses modus ponens or modus tollens.
?
Consider the claim that 'Existence is an accident'. Using the principle of talazum, explain why a composite accident leads to an infinite regress of accidents.

Refuting the Peripatetic Argument on Matter and Individuation
Author recounts the Peripatetic argument that multiplicity requires matter and then refutes it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the Peripatetic argument that multiplicity requires matter, and explain the Illuminationist refutation that the distinguishing factor is not an accidental attribute but the essence after divine making.

Core Idea
The principle of individuation need not be an accident; it can be the essence itself after the perfect maker's act.

In Islamic philosophy, the Peripatetics argued that any essence with multiple individuals must possess matter and accidental distinguishing factors. The author here presents their argument and then refutes it by reinterpreting the principle of individuation, siding with the Illuminationist view. This lesson dissects both the argument and its critique, sharpening your skill in analyzing compound philosophical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Peripatetic thesis
The Peripatetics claim that every essence with multiple individuals (multiplicity) necessarily has matter. Locate this claim in the Arabic text.
2
Extract the premises
List the premises: the essence is common, its necessary attributes are common, that by which it is shared is different from that by which it is distinguished, so the distinguishing factor cannot be the essence or a necessary attribute, hence it is an accidental attribute.
Notice the logical steps leading to the conclusion that the distinguishing factor is an accident.
3
Understand the refutation structure
The author offers two interpretations of 'ما به الامتياز'. First, if it means 'that which gives identity', it refers to the perfect maker (الجاعل التام). Second, if it means 'the source preventing commonality', it is the essence itself after divine making.
The refutation hinges on defining the term; the Peripatetics assumed a specific meaning.
4
Compare the conclusions
The Peripatetics conclude matter is necessary for individuation; the author concludes that the distinguishing factor is either the maker or the essence, requiring no matter. Note the Illuminationist stance.
The author also adds that even if the factor is separate, it could be necessary, not requiring matter.
5
Evaluate logical strength
Assess whether the refutation successfully undermines the Peripatetic argument. Consider whether the Peripatetics could defend their position against these objections.
The refutation identifies an equivocation in 'ما به الامتياز' and provides a coherent alternative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال المشاؤون : كل ماهية متكثرة الأشخاص لا بد لها من مادة ، و اختلاف استعداداتها لأن الماهية مشتركة ، وكذا لوازمها، وما به الاشتراك غير ما به الامتياز، فلا يكون ماهية أو لازما، بل يكون عرضا مفارقا، وهو ممكن الزوال، ففيها قوة ، وهي من العوارض للمادة، ولا يخفى ما فيه ؛ فإنه إن أريد بما به الامتياز مفيدا لهذية، فقد عرفت أنه الجاعل التام، وإنما يحصل الامتياز الشخصي بالجعل، وإن أريد أمر به ينتزع الهذية أي منشأ منع الشركة ، فنختار أنها الماهية والماهية بنفسها مشتركة، وبعد جعل الجاعل ممتازة، فما به الاشتراك نفس ما به الامتياز من غير زيادة أمر فيه على ما هو رأي الإشراقية ، على أنا نقول : يجوز أن يكون ما به الامتياز مفارقا بالنظر إلى الماهية ، لازما للشخص غير ممكن الزوال، فلا يحتاج في عروضها إلى المادة، فتأمل فيه، والله أعلم.
The Peripatetics said: Every essence having multiple persons necessarily has matter, and their potentialities differ because the essence is common, and likewise its necessary attributes, and that by which it is shared is other than that by which it is distinguished; so it is not an essence or necessary attribute but an accidental attribute, and it is liable to cessation; so there is potentiality in it, and it is among the accidents of matter. And what is in it is not hidden; for if by 'that by which it is distinguished' is meant 'that which gives it identity', you know it is the perfect maker, and personal distinction only occurs through making; and if by it is meant something by which identity is derived—i.e., the source preventing commonality—then we choose that it is the essence, and the essence itself is common, and after the maker's making it becomes distinguished; so that by which it is shared is the same as that by which it is distinguished without any added thing in it, according to the view of the Illuminationists. Moreover, we say: It is permissible that that by which it is distinguished is separate from the essence, necessary for the person, not liable to cessation; so it does not need matter for its occurrence. Reflect on it, and God knows best.
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage presents a technical philosophical argument by the Peripatetics (المشاؤون) concerning the need for matter in any essence that has multiple individuals. The argument proceeds: For any essence with many individuals, there must be matter and differing potentialities because the essence is shared among them. Likewise its necessary attributes are shared. That which causes sharing (ما به الاشتراك) is different from that which causes distinction (ما به الامتياز). The distinguishing factor cannot be the essence itself or a necessary attribute, because those are shared; so it must be an accidental attribute (عرض مفارق) that is liable to cessation. Therefore, there is potentiality (قوة) in the individual, and this potentiality is an accident of matter. The author then refutes this: If 'that by which it is distinguished' means 'that which gives identity' (مفيد للهذية), then it is the perfect maker (الجاعل التام), and personal distinction is achieved through divine making, not through matter. If it means 'that by which identity is derived' (منشأ منع الشركة), then we choose that it is the essence itself. The essence is common by itself, but after the maker's making, it becomes distinguished; so that by which it is shared is identical to that by which it is distinguished, with no added element—this is the view of the Illuminationists (الإشراقية). Furthermore, the author says that it is possible that the distinguishing factor is separate from the essence (matter) without being an accidental attribute, and it can be necessary for the person and not liable to cessation, so no matter is needed. The instruction 'فتأمل فيه' (reflect on it) invites the reader to ponder the refutation. This lesson guards against the misconception that individuation always requires a separate accidental cause.

Key Terms
المشاؤون (al-mashshā’ūn): The Peripatetic philosophers; here they present an argument about matter and individuation. [Note: In Islamic philosophy, 'mashsha'ūn' specifically refers to followers of Aristotle; later philosophers may critique them.]
ما به الامتياز (mā bihi al-imtiyāz): That by which a thing is distinguished from others; the principle of individuation. [Note: Stable term, but its interpretation varies between Peripatetics and Illuminationists.]
الجاعل التام (al-jā‘il al-tāmm): The perfect maker; the efficient cause that gives identity to a thing. [Note: More common in kalam and falsafa; in logic it is used sparingly.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Peripatetic argument presented, what is the nature of the distinguishing factor (ما به الامتياز)?
A) It is the essence itself.
B) It is a necessary attribute.
C) It is an accidental attribute that can cease.
D) It is the matter itself.

Key Takeaway
The Peripatetic claim that individuation requires matter is refuted by showing that the distinguishing factor can be the essence itself after divine making, not an accident.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reconstruct the logical structure of the Peripatetic argument in your own words, identifying each premise and the conclusion. Then, in one paragraph, explain how the author's refutation exposes a flaw in the argument.

God as the Only True Existent and the World as Determinations
Theological discourse on the ontological status of God and the world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between God's self-subsistent existence and the derived existence of the world as determinations, and explain how this affects logical predication.

Core Idea
God alone is true existence; the world is only a set of determinations of His essence.

This passage from a logic textbook steps back from formal logic into theological metaphysics. It presents a foundational view that shapes how logic's categories (universal, particular, existence) relate to the divine. Understanding this helps you see why Islamic logicians sometimes insisted on a special treatment of terms like 'existence' when applied to God.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central claim about the Necessary Being
Read the first few lines: 'the Necessary Being, exalted is He, exists; for He is pure existence, and He is existence by Himself, and what is other than Him is not a thing.' The author asserts that God is the only true existent.
2
Analyze the concept of 'determinations' (ta'ayyunat)
Find where the author says the whole world is 'determinations for Him.' Note that the world is not independently existent but a manifestation or individuation of the divine essence.
The term is Sufi, but it functions here to assert ontological dependence.
3
Understand the implication for logical categories
The author says God is 'not a universal nor a particular.' In logic, universals are predicated of many, particulars are not. God's existence transcends these categories. This challenges a purely univocal notion of existence.
This directly impacts how you treat terms like 'existent' in logical arguments about God.
4
Synthesize the concept of absolute existence
God is 'absolute existence' without restriction. The author warns not to make 'absoluteness' a quality. This means God's existence is not limited by any condition, including that of being a universal or particular.
This is a nuanced point: absolute existence is not a universal genus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولتنوّر الكتاب بذكر الحكمة الحقة التي أوحي بها الرسل الكرام المخبرون الصادقون، وكوشف بها أولياء الله العظام، فاعلم أن الواجب سبحانه موجود ; إذ هو وجود بحت، وهو الوجود بنفسه، وما سواه ليس بشيء، والعالم كله شيون له تعالى وتعينات ،له جل مجده. وهو تعالى ظاهر فيه ، ونفسه واجب جاعل المتعينات ممكنة مجعولة في العين الواحد، ظهر في الكثرة، والكثرة إنما تغايره بالاعتبارات، ووجودها مستفاد منه، والموجود حقيقة هو سبحانه، فهو سبحانه وجود مطلق، لا بأن يكون الإطلاق قيدا فيه، ليس بكلي ولا جزئي ولا كثير، ولا شيء معه سواه، ولا شريك له في الوجود، وهو حق منزه في نفسه، وواجب بالذات، وهو خلق مشبه بتعينات عدمية، والواجب لا يكون ممكنا ولا الممكن واجبا، فهو جامع بين التشبيه
And so that the book may be illuminated by mentioning the true wisdom that the noble messengers, truthful informants, were inspired with, and that was unveiled to God's great saints, know that the Necessary Being, exalted is He, exists; for He is pure existence, and He is existence by Himself, and what is other than Him is not a thing, and the whole world is a [set of] determinations for Him, exalted. And He, exalted, is manifest in it, and His very self necessitates that the determinations are possible and made in the single eye, He appeared in multiplicity, and multiplicity differs from Him only in considerations, and their existence is derived from Him, and the true existent is He, exalted. So He, exalted, is absolute existence, not such that absoluteness is a restriction in Him, He is not a universal nor a particular nor many, and nothing is with Him besides Him, and He has no partner in existence, and He is truth transcendent in Himself, and necessary by essence, and He created an entity resembling non-existent determinations, and the necessary cannot be possible nor the possible necessary, so He combines between similarity and transcendence.
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage is a digression into what the author calls 'true wisdom' (الحكمة الحقة), presumably to illuminate the book. It is a theological exposition that draws on Sufi and philosophical ideas. The author states that the Necessary Being (الواجب) is pure existence and the only true existent (الموجود حقيقة هو سبحانه). Everything else is a determination (تعين) of Him. He is absolute existence (وجود مطلق) without the restriction of absoluteness being a quality—He is neither a universal nor a particular. He is transcendent beyond comparisons, yet He created the world resembling non-existent determinations (تعينات عدمية). This combines similarities (تشبيه) with transcendence (تنزيه). This passage is likely meant to provide a metaphysical grounding for the logical discussions, showing that logic's categories are derivative from the divine reality. It is not a standard logical teaching but a theological supplement. For example, the universal concept 'existence' is predicated of many, but God's existence is unique.

Key Terms
الواجب
التعينات
وجود مطلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the ontological status of the world relative to God?
A) The world is a separate creation existing independently.
B) The world is a set of determinations (تعينات) of God, deriving existence from Him.
C) The world is an illusion with no reality.
D) The world is a copy of Platonic forms.

Key Takeaway
God's existence is fundamentally different from all other existence: He alone is true existence, and everything else is merely a determination of His being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the logical rule that a universal term can be predicated of many particulars. How does the author's claim that God is 'not a universal nor a particular' challenge this rule? Write a short paragraph explaining why the standard logical treatment of universals cannot apply to God.
